Proof that a Simple Cause Cannot Produce Multiple Effects
Al-Amidi argues that a truly simple cause can only produce one effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that a cause which is one in all respects cannot give rise to multiple distinct effects.

Core Idea
A truly simple cause lacks the diversity needed to produce multiple effects.

Al-Amidi presents a crisp logical argument that challenges the notion of a purely simple cause producing multiple effects. The key lies in the concept of a 'specific relation' that must exist uniquely between a cause and each of its effects. This passage builds on earlier discussions of causality and unity, pushing us to think carefully about what it means for an agent to be truly one.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Carefully read the Arabic and its translation. Notice the technical term 'خصوصية' (specific relation) and how it is used.
2
Understand the proof structure
The argument proceeds: if a cause is truly one, it cannot have two different specific relations. Therefore, for two effects to proceed, the cause must have some differentiation.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فلا بد من تغاير في ذات الفاعل ولو بالاعتبار'.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Al-Amidi says this judgment is 'close to clarity'. Why might people dispute it?
The common misunderstanding relates to neglecting unity 'with respect to aspects'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : استناد آثار متعددة إلى مؤثر واحد
لها تلك الخصوصية مع غيره؛ إذ لولاها لم يكن اقتضاؤها لمعلول معين بأولى من اقتضائها لما عداه فلا يتصور حينئذ صدوره عنها، ففي كل صدور لا بد أن يكون للمصدر قبل ذلك الصدور خصوصية مع الصادر ليست له مع غيره، المراد بالمصدرية هي هذه الخصوصية لا الأمر الإضافي الذي يتعقل بين الصادر ومصدره؛ لأنه متأخر عنهما، فإذا فرض أن الفاعل واحد حقيقي، وصدر عنه أثر واحد كانت تلك الخصوصية بحسب ذات الفاعل، وإن فرض صدور أثر آخر كانت تلك الخصوصية الذات؛ إذ ليس هناك جهة أخرى، فلا يكون له مع شيء من المعلولين خصوصية ليست له مع غيره فلا يكون علة لشيء منهما فإذا تعدد المعلول، فلا بد من تغاير في ذات الفاعل ولو بالاعتبار ؛ ليتصور هناك خصوصيتان تترتب عليهما عليتان . وحينئذ لا يكون الفاعل واحداً من جميع الجهات، ولهذا قيل : إن هذا الحكم كأنه قريب من الوضوح، وإنما كثرت مدافعة الناس إياه لإغفالهم عن معنى الوحدة أيضاً بحسب الجهات، سواء كان موجوداً أو لا.
The Fifth Observational Chapter - The Third Objective: The attribution of multiple effects to a single cause. It has that specific relation with it without the other; for without it, its requiring a particular effect would not be more appropriate than its requiring what is other than it, so then its proceeding from it would not be conceivable. So in every procession, there must be for the source before that procession a specific relation with the proceeding thing that it does not have with another. The intended meaning of sourcehood is this specific relation, not the relational matter that is understood between the proceeding thing and its source, because it is posterior to both. So if the agent is assumed to be truly one, and one effect proceeds from it, that specific relation is with respect to the agent's essence. But if another effect is assumed to issue, that specific relation would be with the essence, since there is no other aspect. Then it would not have with any of the two effects a specific relation that it does not have with the other, so it would not be a cause for either of them. Thus if the effect is multiple, there must be a difference in the agent's essence, even if by consideration, so that two specific relations can be conceived upon which two causalities depend. Then the agent is not one from all aspects. Therefore it is said that this judgment is, as it were, close to clarity, and people's disputation about it is only due to their heedlessness of the meaning of unity also with respect to aspects, whether it exists or not.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that a truly simple cause cannot produce multiple effects. The proof revolves around the concept of 'specific relation' (خصوصية). For an effect to proceed from a cause, there must be a specific relation between them that distinguishes it from other potential effects. If the cause is one in all respects, it cannot have distinct specific relations to multiple effects, as that would imply some multiplicity in the cause. Therefore, if multiple effects occur, the cause must have some sort of differentiation, even if only conceptual, making it not absolutely simple. The author notes that this argument is almost obvious, yet many dispute it because they neglect the full meaning of unity considered from all aspects.

Key Terms
خصوصية
مصدرية
فاعل
معلول
الوحدة الحقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God can create multiple things directly because He is simple.
Correction: The argument only applies to a cause that is one in every respect without any internal distinction. In Islamic theology, God's essence encompasses attributes and relations that allow for multiple acts of creation without compromising unity in a way that implies composition.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude must be the case if a single cause produces multiple effects?
A) The cause must be absolutely simple.
B) There must be a difference in the cause's essence, even if by consideration.
C) The cause cannot be an agent.
D) Multiple effects are impossible.

Key Takeaway
A cause that is one in every respect cannot be the source of multiple distinct effects; multiplicity of effects necessitates some form of differentiation in the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you treat God as if He is too 'simple' to care about the details of your struggles? Have you ever doubted that He can attend to each of your needs with a distinct and personal relation? This argument shows that multiplicity does not force simplicity—your many needs do not overwhelm Him. Will you change how you approach Him with each specific need, trusting that He has a unique relation to every situation?
?
Al-Amidi asks you to consider that for any effect to occur, a specific relation must exist. Think of the effects you want to see in your own character: patience, gratitude, sincerity. Are you merely waiting for them to happen, or are you actively cultivating a specific relation with the Source? What concrete step will you take today to tie your soul more directly to the One Who gives these gifts?
?
The argument concludes that unity does not mean lack of distinction in the cause. How often do you insist on a 'simple' understanding of God—ignoring the rich distinctions in His names and attributes? Reflect: Do you reduce God to a single abstract concept, or do you embrace the fullness of His nature as revealed in His creation and revelation? What would it mean for your worship to truly honor the many specific relations He holds with all things?

Objection and Response on Causal Specificity
Al-Amidi presents an objection against the necessity of specific relation and responds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the necessity of a specific relation in causality and how it is refuted within the kalam framework.

Core Idea
The specific relation is not a real property but a description of necessary particularization.

This lesson examines a critical objection in the theology of causality: the claim that the specific relation between cause and effect is not a real attribute of the cause but merely its role as a particularizer. Al-Amidi presents this objection and then responds by arguing that the specific relation is necessary to avoid the problem of preponderance without a preferrer. The discussion engages deeper arguments, including one by al-Dawani about the impossibility of distinguishing effects when the specific relation is shared.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Look for the phrase that introduces the objection: 'على أنه يرد عليه منع كونها فاعلة في الحقيقة'.
The objection claims the cause is only a particularizer, not a true agent with a specific relation.
2
Understand the response and the reasoning from preponderance
Notice how the response argues that without a specific relation, preponderance without a preferrer would occur. This is a key kalam principle.
3
Examine al-Dawani's counter-argument
Al-Dawani argues that if the specific relation is shared, no effect can be uniquely determined. Identify his reasoning about the common denominator.
This challenges the idea that a single cause can produce multiple distinct effects.
4
Consider the final reply based on the principle that a particular effect requires only some cause
The reply states that a particular effect requires only a cause in general, not a specific aspect. This is supported by later verification.
5
Reflect on the significance for kalam causality
Consider how this debate shapes the understanding of causal efficacy and divine action in Ash'ari kalam.
The specific relation safeguards divine power while avoiding preponderance without a preferrer.

على أنه يرد عليه منع كونها فاعلة في الحقيقة؛ لأنها جميع مخصصة لوقوع المعلول على النحو الخاص. قوله: (فإذا فرض إلخ) و به اندفع الجواب الذي نقلنا من أنه لو تم لامتنع صدور الأثر الواحد منه أيضاً. · قوله : ( فلا يكون له مع شيء من المعلولين (خصوصية فيه أن اللازم مما سبق أن يكون للعلة خصوصية بمعنى أن يقتضي وجود المعلول على النحو الخاص؛ لئلا يلزم الترجيح بلا مرجح وأما أن تكون تلك مختصة بكل معلول بمعنى أن لا يكون مع معلول آخر، فكلا هذا حاصل الجواب المذكور بقوله : قلنا إلخ. وبما ذكرنا اندفع ما قاله المحقق الدواني من أنه إذا اشتركت الخصوصية في الجميع، ولم يتحقق ما يختص بكل واحد لم يتحقق منشأ خصوصية كل واحد وهويته التي يمتاز بها عن غيره فتلك الخصوصية لو اقتضت شيئاً اقتضت القدر المشترك، فلم يتحقق الأمور المتعددة المتغايرة . أن فيقتضي ماهية كل من المعلولين أن يوجد بإيجاد تلك العلة البسيطة، كما في الأنواع المنحصرة كل منها في شخص، فلا يلزم تعدد جهات العلة المذكورة قلت لما تقرر عندهم من المعلول المعين لا يقتضي إلا علة ما، كما سيأتي تحقيقه.
But there is an objection rejecting its being truly an agent, because it is entirely a particularizer for the occurrence of the effect in a specific manner. His saying: (if it is assumed etc.) and by it is refuted the response we transmitted, that if it were perfect, the occurrence of a single effect from it would also be impossible. His saying: (so it does not have with any of the two effects a specific relation) in it is that the necessary consequence from what preceded is that the cause has a specific relation in the sense that it necessitates the existence of the effect in a specific manner, to avoid preponderance without a preferrer. As for that being specific to each effect in the sense that it is not with another effect, that is all the result of the mentioned response by his saying: we said etc. And by what we mentioned is refuted what al-Muhaqqiq al-Dawani said: that if the specific relation is shared in all, and what is specific to each does not obtain, then the source of each one's specificity and its identity by which it is distinguished from others does not obtain; so that specific relation, if it necessitated something, would necessitate the common denominator, so the multiple differing things would not obtain. That it necessitates the essence of each of the two effects to exist by the existence of that simple cause, as in the species each of which is restricted to one individual, so the multiplicity of aspects of the mentioned cause does not follow. I say because it is established with them that a particular effect requires only some cause, as will be verified later.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and a response concerning the necessity of a specific relation. The objector argues that the specific relation is not a real property of the cause but merely a description of its role as a particularizer. The author responds by insisting that the specific relation is necessary to avoid preponderance without a preferrer. He then addresses an argument by al-Dawani that if the specific relation is shared across all effects, then no effect can be uniquely determined. The author replies that, according to their principles, a particular effect requires only a cause in general, not necessarily a cause with multiple aspects.

Correction: The objection clarifies that the specific relation is merely the cause's being a particularizer; it is not an additional attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the objection here?
A) The specific relation is impossible.
B) The specific relation is not a real property but a description of particularization.
C) The cause must be multiple in itself.
D) Multiple effects require multiple causes.

Key Takeaway
The specific relation is not an added property but the cause's role as particularizer, necessary to avoid preponderance without a preferrer.

Distinguishing تخلف from اختلاف in Causal Proof
Jurjani distinguishes تخلف from اختلاف as evidence for multiple causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between failure of effect (تخلف) and mere difference (اختلاف) as evidence for multiple causes, and why Jurjani argues that only تخلف proves diversity of causes.

Core Idea
Multiple effects do not imply multiple causes; only failure of one effect from another's cause does.

In this passage, Jurjani responds to the second objection against his position on causality. The objection claims that if one cause produces multiple different effects, then those effects must come from different causes. Jurjani sharpens the argument by distinguishing between two types of evidence: mere difference in effects (اختلاف) versus failure of one effect to appear from the other cause (تخلف). He argues that only تخلف can prove that causes are distinct.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the key terms تخلف and اختلاف. See how they are contrasted.
Pay attention to the example of fire and water.
2
Understand the example of fire and water
Jurjani uses fire and water: fire does not produce cold (the effect of water), and water does not produce heat (the effect of fire). This failure (تخلف) indicates their natures are different.
3
Grasp the logical principle
Without تخلف, mere multiplicity of effects does not prove multiplicity of causes. A single cause could produce different effects under different conditions. That is the point in dispute.
The commentary summarizes the further discussion and refutation of counterarguments.
4
Reflect on the commentary
Read the commentary summary and note how it defends the position against potential objections about accidental causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن الثاني: أن الاستدلال على تغاير طبيعتي الماء والنار (إنما هو بالتخلف، لا بالاختلاف والتعدد ) فإنا لما رأينا ناراً ولا برد معها كما كان مع رأينا ( ماء ولا حر معه كما كان مع النار ( علمنا ) بتخلف أثر كل منهما عن ( أنهما مختلفان ) ؛ إذ لو تساويا لامتنع تخلف الأثر فلو رأينا آثارا مختلفة متعددة بلا تخلف لم يمكن لنا الاستدلال بها على اختلاف المؤثرات وتعددها، بل هذا هو المتنازع فيه،. قوله : ( والجواب عن الثاني إلخ خلاصته : منع كون الاستدلال على التعدد بالاختلاف لم يجوز أن يكون بالتخلف فالمناقشة فيه بأن التخلف لا يثبت بتغايرهما بالطبيعة لجواز أن يكون بسببين ،عارضين ويكون علة العارضين الأمر المشترك بانضمام بعض الاعتبارات، أو يكون العوارض متسلسلة غير مجتمعة الوجود كالاستعدادات كلام على السند الغير المساوي، على أن تلك المناقشة مدفوعة كما فصل في مبحث إثبات الصورة النوعية .
And the answer to the second [objection] is that the inference to the difference of the two natures of water and fire is only by failure [of effect] (تخلف), not by difference and multiplicity. For when we saw fire with no cold with it, as we saw water with no heat with it as was with fire, we knew by the failure of each's effect that they are different; for if they were equivalent, failure of effect would be impossible. So if we saw different multiple effects without failure, we could not infer different and multiple causes; rather, that is the point in dispute. His statement: (And the answer to the second etc. Its summary is: denial that the inference to multiplicity is by difference; it is not permissible that it be by failure. The discussion about it that failure is not established by their difference in nature, because it is possible that it be due to two accidental causes, and the cause of the accidents is the common thing with the addition of some considerations, or that the accidents are successive, not coexisting, like dispositions, is speech on non-equivalent ground, though that discussion is refuted as detailed in the section on proving the specific form.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the second objection. The objection claimed that the coexistence of multiple different effects from one cause implies multiple causes. The author responds by distinguishing between two ways to infer multiple causes: by difference (اختلاف) and by failure of effect (تخلف). He uses the example of fire and water: we know they are different natures not because they produce different effects, but because fire does not produce the effect of water (cold) and water does not produce the effect of fire (heat). That is failure of effect. Without such failure, mere difference in effects does not prove multiple causes; the cause could be one and the same but produce different effects under different conditions. The commentary then summarizes that the opponent’s argument is denied, and further discussion about the possibility of accidental causes is addressed but refuted.

Key Terms
تخلف
اختلاف
تغاير
طبيعتين
المؤثرات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two effects are different, they must come from different causes.
Correction: Mere difference in effects does not prove multiplicity of causes; only when one cause fails to produce the other's effect (تخلف) does it indicate distinct natures.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, how do we know that fire and water have different natures?
A) Because fire produces heat and water produces cold.
B) Because fire fails to produce the effect of water (cold) and water fails to produce the effect of fire (heat).
C) Because they are opposites.
D) Because they have different colors.

Key Takeaway
The proof for multiple causes lies in the failure of one cause to produce the effect of another, not in the mere diversity of effects.

Would you be willing to erase those rivals and let every effect, whether pleasant or painful, be the direct result of His will alone? What step can you take today to act on that conviction?

Al-Katibi's Argument: Modified Proportion Entails Simple Negative
Al-Katibi's argument and the logical entailment between modified positive and simple negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Katibi's argument that producing non-(a) entails non-production of (a), and the logical relation between modified positive and simple negative propositions.

Core Idea
Producing what is not (a) logically entails not producing (a).

Al-Katibi presents a concise argument: if a source produces something other than (a), then it is true that it did not produce (a), leading to a contradiction. To analyze this, the commentator explores the logical relationship between a modified positive proposition ('occurrence of non-(a)') and a simple negative proposition ('non-occurrence of (a)'). This lesson unpacks that relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Katibi's claim
Read the Arabic carefully. Al-Katibi argues that producing (b) (which is not (a)) implies that (a) did not issue from that source, resulting in a contradiction.
Notice the logical leap from 'producing (b)' to 'non-production of (a)'.
2
Understand the logical relation
The commentator explains that the entailment is mediated by the relation between a modified positive proposition ('occurrence of non-(a)') and a simple negative ('non-occurrence of (a)').
3
Apply the principle
Just as a modified positive (e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing') entails a simple negative ('Zayd does not see'), the modified positive here entails the simple negative.
This resolves the earlier objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به قال الكاتبي في شرح الملخص: إذا صدر عنه (ب) الذي هو غیر (۱) من تلك الجهة صدق أنه لم يصدر عنه (۱) من تلك الجهة، فيصدق حينئذ أنه صدر عنه (ا) ولم يصدر عنه (۱) من جهة واحدة، وأنه تناقض وهذا الوجه كتبه الرئيس إلى بهمنيار لما طلب منه البرهان على هذا المطلوب ثم قال : جوابه لا نسلم أنه إذا صدر عنه (ب) صدق أنه لم يصدر عنه (۱) بل اللازم أنه ... إلخ أن النسبة التقييدية التي اعتبر متعلقها بطريق العدول أعني صدور لا (ا) استلزامه للنسبة السلبية التي اعتبر متعلقها بطريق التحصيل، أعني سلب صدور (۱) كاستلزام الموجبة المعدولة للسالبة المحصلة إذا كانت النسبة الإيجابية المعدولة مستلزمة للنسبة السلبية المحصلة، سواء كانتا خبريتين أو تقييديتين، وعلى هذا التقرير يندفع إيراد الشارح قدس سره بأنه سهو؛ لأن إلخ . نعم يرد عليه أن صدق سلب صدور (۱) على صدور (ب)، لا يقتضي اتصاف الجهة بذلك السلب حتى يلزم التناقض، فإن السواد الذي في الجسم يصدق عليه أنه ليس بجسم، ولا جوهر، ولا متحيز مع امتناع اتصاف الجسم بها، ومن هذا ظهر ركاكة ما قاله المحقق الدواني من أن صدور لا ( أ ) ليس صدور (۱) فهو لاصدور (۱) فما اتصف بصدور لا (۱) فقد اتصف بلا صدور (۱) فإن كان له حيثيتان جاز أن يكون متصفاً من حيثية بصدور (۱) ومن حيثية أخرى بلا صدور (۱) من غير تناقض، وأما إذا لم يكن إلا حيثية واحدة لم يصح أن يتصف بهما؛ للزوم التناقض . وعند هذا ظهر انعكاس تشنيع الإمام على الشيخ .
Al-Katibi said in his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas: When (b) which is other than (a) issues from that source, it is true that (a) did not issue from that source. Then it is true that (a) issued and did not issue from one source, and that is a contradiction. And this aspect was written by the Chief (al-Ra'is) to Bahmanyar when he asked for a proof on this sought issue. Then he said: Its answer is we do not grant that when (b) issues from it, it is true that (a) did not issue from it; rather the necessary is that... etc., that the relative proportion (al-nisba al-taqyīdiyya) whose relation is considered by way of modification, I mean the occurrence of non-(a), entails the negative proportion whose relation is considered by way of simple (taḥṣīl), I mean the negation of occurrence of (a), just as the modified positive entails the simple negative, when the modified positive proportion entails the simple negative proportion, whether they are declarative (khabariyya) or relative (taqyīdiyya). And on this formulation, the objection of the commentator (quddisa sirruh) that it is a mistake falls away. Yes, it may be objected that the truth of the negation of occurrence of (a) for the occurrence of (b) does not necessitate the attribute of the source with that negation such that contradiction follows. For the blackness that is in a body is truly described as not a body, not a substance, not extended, while it is impossible for the body to be described by these. From this appears the weakness of what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī said: that the occurrence of non-(a) is not the occurrence of (a), so it is non-occurrence of (a). Whoever is described by the occurrence of non-(a) is described by non-occurrence of (a). If it has two aspects (ḥaythiyyatān), it may be described from one aspect by the occurrence of (a) and from another aspect by non-occurrence of (a) without contradiction. But if there is only one aspect, it is not valid for it to be described by both, due to the necessity of contradiction. And at this point, the reversal of the Imam’s condemnation of the Shaykh becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents al-Katibi’s version of the argument: if a source produces (b) which is not (a), then it is true that (a) did not issue from that source. This results in both production and non-production of (a) from the same source, a contradiction. The Chief (Ibn Sina) wrote this to Bahmanyar. The commentator then explains that the answer is not to grant that producing (b) entails non-production of (a). Then he analyzes the logical relation: the modified proportion 'occurrence of non-(a)' entails the simple negative 'negation of occurrence of (a)'. This is like a modified positive entailing a simple negative. On this basis, the earlier objection of the commentator that it is a mistake is refuted. However, the commentator raises a further point: the truth of the negative predication (e.g., 'it is true that (a) did not occur') does not mean the source actually possesses the attribute of non-occurrence. For example, blackness in a body can be truly said to be not a body, not a substance, etc., but the body itself does not possess those negations as real attributes. Similarly, the source does not become actually characterized by the negation. Al-Dawani’s claim that if the source has two aspects it can have both attributes without contradiction is weak because the issue is about a single aspect. The commentator concludes that this clarifies the reversal of the Imam’s criticism of the Shaykh.

Key Terms
نسبة تقييدية
نسبة سلبية
موجبة معدولة
سالبة محصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The inference from 'producing (b)' to 'non-production of (a)' is straightforward and needs no justification.
Correction: The commentator shows that the entailment is mediated by the logical relation between modified positive and simple negative proportions.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, the modified positive 'occurrence of non-(a)' entails which of the following?
A) Occurrence of (a)
B) Non-occurrence of (a)
C) Both occurrence and non-occurrence
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The modified positive proposition 'occurrence of non-(a)' logically entails the simple negative 'non-occurrence of (a)', resolving the apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about what God does or does not do, do you ever fall into the trap of assuming that because He does one thing, He cannot do another, without carefully considering the logical form of your statements? How can al-Katibi's distinction between a modified positive and a simple negative refine your understanding of divine actions?
?
This lesson shows that even subtle shifts in wording—between 'occurrence of non-(a)' and 'non-occurrence of (a)'—carry different logical implications. Have you ever expressed a belief about God in a way that was logically imprecise, leading to confusion or contradiction? What specific step will you take to be more precise in your theological language starting today?
?
The commentator initially rejected al-Katibi's argument as a mistake. Have you ever dismissed a theological or logical point too quickly because it seemed counterintuitive, only to later realize you missed a nuance? Can you identify a belief you hold where you might be making a similar mistake, and commit to re-examining it in light of this lesson's principle?

Negative Predication and Real Attributes
Objection that negative predication does not imply real attribute, with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the truth of a negative predication does not imply a real negative attribute in the subject, using the example of blackness and body.

Core Idea
A negative proposition can be true without the subject having a real negative attribute.

Building on al-Katibi's argument, the commentator raises a crucial objection: even if it is true to say that (a) did not occur, this does not mean the source actually possesses the attribute of non-occurrence. The example of blackness in a body illustrates that negative predications can be true without conferring real attributes. This lesson examines that objection and the refutation of al-Dawani's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'نعم يرد عليه'. The commentator objects that truth of a negative predication does not require a real attribute in the subject.
2
Understand the blackness analogy
Consider the example: blackness in a body is truly described as not a body, not a substance, etc., but the body itself is not actually described by these negations.
This shows that negative predications can be true without the subject having a real negative attribute.
3
Analyze al-Dawani's view
Al-Dawani claims that if the source has two aspects, it can have both attributes without contradiction. The commentator implies this is misguided because the issue concerns a single aspect.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The passage ends with a note that this analysis reverses the Imam's criticism of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
به قال الكاتبي في شرح الملخص: إذا صدر عنه (ب) الذي هو غیر (۱) من تلك الجهة صدق أنه لم يصدر عنه (۱) من تلك الجهة، فيصدق حينئذ أنه صدر عنه (ا) ولم يصدر عنه (۱) من جهة واحدة، وأنه تناقض وهذا الوجه كتبه الرئيس إلى بهمنيار لما طلب منه البرهان على هذا المطلوب ثم قال : جوابه لا نسلم أنه إذا صدر عنه (ب) صدق أنه لم يصدر عنه (۱) بل اللازم أنه ... إلخ أن النسبة التقييدية التي اعتبر متعلقها بطريق العدول أعني صدور لا (ا) استلزامه للنسبة السلبية التي اعتبر متعلقها بطريق التحصيل، أعني سلب صدور (۱) كاستلزام الموجبة المعدولة للسالبة المحصلة إذا كانت النسبة الإيجابية المعدولة مستلزمة للنسبة السلبية المحصلة، سواء كانتا خبريتين أو تقييديتين، وعلى هذا التقرير يندفع إيراد الشارح قدس سره بأنه سهو؛ لأن إلخ . نعم يرد عليه أن صدق سلب صدور (۱) على صدور (ب)، لا يقتضي اتصاف الجهة بذلك السلب حتى يلزم التناقض، فإن السواد الذي في الجسم يصدق عليه أنه ليس بجسم، ولا جوهر، ولا متحيز مع امتناع اتصاف الجسم بها، ومن هذا ظهر ركاكة ما قاله المحقق الدواني من أن صدور لا ( أ ) ليس صدور (۱) فهو لاصدور (۱) فما اتصف بصدور لا (۱) فقد اتصف بلا صدور (۱) فإن كان له حيثيتان جاز أن يكون متصفاً من حيثية بصدور (۱) ومن حيثية أخرى بلا صدور (۱) من غير تناقض، وأما إذا لم يكن إلا حيثية واحدة لم يصح أن يتصف بهما؛ للزوم التناقض . وعند هذا ظهر انعكاس تشنيع الإمام على الشيخ .
Al-Katibi said in his commentary on al-Mulakhkhas: When (b) which is other than (a) issues from that source, it is true that (a) did not issue from that source. Then it is true that (a) issued and did not issue from one source, and that is a contradiction. And this aspect was written by the Chief (al-Ra'is) to Bahmanyar when he asked for a proof on this sought issue. Then he said: Its answer is we do not grant that when (b) issues from it, it is true that (a) did not issue from it; rather the necessary is that... etc., that the relative proportion (al-nisba al-taqyīdiyya) whose relation is considered by way of modification, I mean the occurrence of non-(a), entails the negative proportion whose relation is considered by way of simple (taḥṣīl), I mean the negation of occurrence of (a), just as the modified positive entails the simple negative, when the modified positive proportion entails the simple negative proportion, whether they are declarative (khabariyya) or relative (taqyīdiyya). And on this formulation, the objection of the commentator (quddisa sirruh) that it is a mistake falls away. Yes, it may be objected that the truth of the negation of occurrence of (a) for the occurrence of (b) does not necessitate the attribute of the source with that negation such that contradiction follows. For the blackness that is in a body is truly described as not a body, not a substance, not extended, while it is impossible for the body to be described by these. From this appears the weakness of what al-Muḥaqqiq al-Dawānī said: that the occurrence of non-(a) is not the occurrence of (a), so it is non-occurrence of (a). Whoever is described by the occurrence of non-(a) is described by non-occurrence of (a). If it has two aspects (ḥaythiyyatān), it may be described from one aspect by the occurrence of (a) and from another aspect by non-occurrence of (a) without contradiction. But if there is only one aspect, it is not valid for it to be described by both, due to the necessity of contradiction. And at this point, the reversal of the Imam’s condemnation of the Shaykh becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage presents al-Katibi’s version of the argument: if a source produces (b) which is not (a), then it is true that (a) did not issue from that source. This results in both production and non-production of (a) from the same source, a contradiction. The Chief (Ibn Sina) wrote this to Bahmanyar. The commentator then explains that the answer is not to grant that producing (b) entails non-production of (a). Then he analyzes the logical relation: the modified proportion 'occurrence of non-(a)' entails the simple negative 'negation of occurrence of (a)'. This is like a modified positive entailing a simple negative. On this basis, the earlier objection of the commentator that it is a mistake is refuted. However, the commentator raises a further point: the truth of the negative predication (e.g., 'it is true that (a) did not occur') does not mean the source actually possesses the attribute of non-occurrence. For example, blackness in a body can be truly said to be not a body, not a substance, etc., but the body itself does not possess those negations as real attributes. Similarly, the source does not become actually characterized by the negation. Al-Dawani’s claim that if the source has two aspects it can have both attributes without contradiction is weak because the issue is about a single aspect. The commentator concludes that this clarifies the reversal of the Imam’s criticism of the Shaykh.

Key Terms
اتصاف انتزاعي
حيثية
سلب خبري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a negative proposition is true, the subject must possess the corresponding negative attribute.
Correction: The example of blackness shows that a subject (body) does not acquire the attributes of 'not being a body' etc., even though blackness is truly described as such.

Checkpoint
What does the example of blackness in a body demonstrate?
A) That negative predications always imply real attributes.
B) That a negative proposition can be true without the subject having that negative attribute.
C) That blackness is not a substance.
D) That the source has two aspects.

Key Takeaway
The truth of a negative predication does not entail a real negative attribute in the subject; this principle protects al-Katibi's argument from refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a negative statement you often say about yourself, like 'I am not patient' or 'I am not generous'. Does its truth in a specific situation mean that 'impatience' is a real attribute of your soul? How would your self-view change if you distinguished between a true factual description and a fixed negative attribute?
?
Al-Dawani thought that having two 'aspects' could allow contradictory descriptions without contradiction. In your own life, when you hold conflicting beliefs or behaviors, do you mentally compartmentalize them into different 'aspects' of yourself? What would it mean to face the unity of your self and resolve those contradictions rather than hide behind aspects?
?
The passage shows that the truth of a negation does not make the subject acquire that negation as a real attribute. Are there negative qualities—like 'unworthy' or 'failure'—that you have allowed to define you based on true negative statements about your actions? Could you separate your essence from such factual descriptions, and what relief might that bring?

Al-Razi's Critique and the Quantification of Propositions
Al-Razi's criticism and the quantification of propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the quantification of propositions with perpetuity or absolute terms resolves apparent contradictions, and why al-Razi criticizes scholars who neglect logical analysis in theological debates.

Core Idea
Logic is the protective instrument; neglecting it leads to laughable errors in theology.

Al-Razi introduces a critique against those who master logic but fail to apply it to the noblest issues of theology. This lesson examines how the distinction between absolute and perpetually quantified propositions clarifies why certain statements are not contradictory, and why ignoring this leads to childish mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the core argument
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the heading 'al-Ghalat' (The Error) and the statement that two absolute propositions are not contradictory. If one is quantified with perpetuity, it becomes false.
Pay attention to the role of time in the argument.
2
Understand al-Razi's critique
Al-Razi criticizes those who spend their lives teaching logic but do not apply it to this noble issue, falling into errors that even children would laugh at. Reflect on why he considers this amazing.
3
Examine the answer and its rebuttal
The gloss explains that the truth of absolute propositions is due to different times, but here time is one. The earlier rebuttal showed that the unity of time is not problematic because the negative is conceptual. See how this reinforces the conclusion.
This step connects back to the earlier discussion about unity of time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغلط
صدر عنه ما ليس (۱) ، وإن سلم فلا تناقض بين قولنا صدر عنه (۱) ولم يصدر عنه (۱) لأنهما مطلقتان. وإن قيدت إحداهما بالدوام كانت كاذبة قال الإمام الرازي في المباحث المشرقية والعجب ممن يفني عمره في تعليم الآلة العاصمة وتعلمها، ثم إذا جاء إلى هذا المطلوب الأشرف أعرض عن استعمالها، حتى يقع في غلط يضحك منه الصبيان .
قوله : ( وإن قيدت إحداهما إلخ) أجيب : بأن صدق المطلقتين إنما يكون لاختلاف الزمان فيهما والزمان هاهنا ،واحد ، بناء على فرض كونه علة تامة لكل منهما، وقد عرفت اندفاعه اتحاد الزمان .
The Error: What issued from it is not (a); and if granted, there is no contradiction between our saying 'issued from it (a)' and 'did not issue from it (a)' because they are both absolute (muṭlaqatān). And if one of them is quantified with perpetuity, it would be false. Imam al-Razi said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: Amazing is the one who spends his life in teaching and learning the protective instrument (logic), then when he comes to this nobler sought issue, he turns away from using it, until he falls into an error at which children laugh. His words: (And if one of them is quantified with perpetuity etc.) It was answered: The truth of the two absolute propositions is only due to difference of time in them, while time here is one, based on the assumption that it is a complete cause for each of them, and you have known that the unity of time is rebutted.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage begins with a heading 'The Error' and presents a claim that even if it is granted that what issues from the source is not (a), there is no contradiction because the two propositions are absolute (muṭlaqa) – they are not quantified as perpetual. If one were quantified as perpetual, it would be false. Al-Razi then criticizes those who study logic but fail to apply it to this noble issue, committing childish errors. A gloss then states the answer to a possible objection: the truth of the two absolute propositions is due to difference of time; but here time is unified because the source is a complete cause for both. However, the gloss notes that the unity of time has already been rebutted earlier. So this reinforces the earlier conclusion that the two propositions are not truly contradictory.

Key Terms
مطلقة
دوام
آلة عاصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absolute propositions are always non-contradictory regardless of context.
Correction: Absolute propositions are non-contradictory only when they refer to different times; if time is unified, the contradiction reappears unless the negative is conceptual, as shown by the earlier rebuttal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if one of the absolute propositions is quantified with perpetuity?
A) It becomes true.
B) It becomes false.
C) It remains absolute.
D) It becomes necessary.

Key Takeaway
The passage demonstrates that the distinction between absolute and perpetually quantified propositions is crucial in logical analysis, and al-Razi emphasizes that logic must be applied consistently even in theological arguments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you invested time in learning a skill or knowledge (like logic) but then failed to apply it in a crucial situation? What was at stake?
?
Al-Razi says that neglecting logic leads to errors that even children would laugh at. What area of your faith or understanding are you avoiding because careful reasoning might be uncomfortable?
?
If you were to take al-Razi's criticism personally, what specific change will you make in how you approach theological discussions this week?

Acceptance and Action Are Mental Considerations
Author asserts thesis and gives argument from necessity and possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claim that acceptance and action are not external existents but mental considerations, and the initial argument from necessity and possibility.

Core Idea
Acceptance and action are mental relations, not external realities.

In this passage, the author begins by asserting a key thesis of the discussion: that acceptance and action are not external existents. He then presents a supporting argument based on the distinctions of necessity and possibility in causal relations. This lesson introduces that foundational claim and its first evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Assertion
Read the opening statement: 'We said: You have known that acceptance and action in the sense of effect are not among external existents.' This is the central claim.
Notice the confident tone: 'You have known' implies prior grounding.
2
Examine the Argument
Then the author adds another argument: 'The relation of the agent to the effect is by necessity, and the relation of the receiver to the received is by possibility, so they do not coincide.' Identify the two relations and how they differ.
This argument is meant to show that agency and reception are distinct, so they cannot be the same external entity.
3
Reflect on the Conclusion
The conclusion is that acceptance and action are not external existents because they have different modal properties (necessity vs. possibility). Ask yourself: why would this imply they are mental?
The idea is that if they were external, they would coexist, but the modalities conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : ( وقد عرفت ) أن القبول والفعل بمعنى التأثير ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية. أيضاً (جوابه)، مع ( وأيضاً : فنسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان )، فلا يجتمعان واعترض على هذا بأن القابل إذا أخذ وحده لم يجب معه وجود المقبول، وكما أن الفاعل وحده لا يجب معه وجود المفعول، وإذا أخذا مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود المقبول والمفعول وجب وجودهما معهما، فلا فرق إذا بينهما في الوجوب والإمكان. وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده قد يكون في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجبا. قوله: ( وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده إلخ فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد أن المقبول إذا كان مما يجب أن يكون له محل ،قابل كما هو محل النزاع ففاعله قد يكون وحده في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجباً له، فهو ممنوع إذ لا بد له من القابل وإن أراد أن المقبول إذا لم يكن كذلك، ففاعله يجوز أن يكون مستقلاً في بعض الصور بإيجابه فهو مسلم ، لكن لا يلزم من هذا تناف في محل النزاع، إذ لا استقلال لشيء من القابل والفاعل بالإيجاب بالنسبة إلى المفعول والمقبول، ومن شرط التنافي أن يكون حصول المتنافيين بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد على أن في قوله، ولا يتصور في القابل شائبة مصادرة؛ لأن التصديق بهذا القول يتوقف على التصديق بأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً ، وإلا فقد يكون ذلك القابل هو الفاعل، فيكون القابل موجباً للمقبول وحده، فإن قلت : إيجابه ليس من حيث إنه ،قابل، بل من حيث إنه فاعل قلت : هذا إنما يفيد تغاير مفهومي القابل والفاعل، ولا يدل على أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون متصفاً بهذين المفهومين على ما هو، المدعى فتأمل هذا. وقد يدفع جواب الشارح أيضاً بأن إمكان الوجوب إنما هو من جهة الفاعلية، أيضاً . هذا المجيب، وامتناع الوجوب إنما هو من جهة القابلية، كما به صرح فإمكان الوجوب وامتناعه نيسا من جهة واحدة بل من جهتين مختلفتين هما الفاعلية والقابلية ولا محذور في ذلك . وستطلع في المقصد السادس على سقوط هذا الكلام بقي ها هنا شيء وهو كما صرح به
We said: You have known that acceptance and action in the sense of effect are not among external existents. Also, the answer, and also: The relation of the agent to the effect is by necessity, and the relation of the receiver to the received is by possibility, so they do not coincide. An objection was raised against this: When the receiver is taken alone, the existence of the received is not necessitated, just as when the agent alone is taken, the existence of the effect is not necessitated. But when both are taken with all the conditions for existence, their existence becomes necessary. So there is no difference between them in necessity and possibility. The answer was given: The agent alone may in some cases be independently necessitating. His statement: 'And the answer that the agent alone etc. is debatable because if he means that the received, when it must have a place, is the point of contention, then its agent may be independently necessitating in some cases – this is denied because it requires a receiver. If he means that the received is not like that, then its agent may be independently necessitating in some cases – this is granted, but it does not entail conflict in the point of contention, because neither the receiver nor the agent independently necessitates with respect to the effect and the received. The condition of conflict is that the two opposing things occur with respect to a single thing. Moreover, his statement 'There is no consideration of a receiver' contains a begging of the question, because affirming this statement depends on affirming that a single thing cannot be both receiver and agent; otherwise, that receiver might be the agent, so the receiver necessitates the received alone. If you say: Its necessitation is not insofar as it is a receiver but insofar as it is an agent, I say: This only establishes the conceptual distinction between receiver and agent, but does not prove that a single thing cannot be described by both concepts, which is the claim. Consider this. The answer of the commentator may also be refuted by saying that the possibility of necessity is from the aspect of agency, and the impossibility of necessity is from the aspect of reception, as he explicitly stated. So possibility and impossibility are not from one aspect but from two different aspects: agency and reception, and there is no harm in that. You will see in the sixth objective the failure of this argument. There remains something here, which is as he stated.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a key response to the earlier objection about the simple being. It begins by stating that acceptance and action (in the sense of effect) are not external existents but mental considerations (إضافات). Then it presents another argument: the relation of agent to effect is by necessity, while the relation of receiver to received is by possibility, so they cannot coexist. An objector argues that when all conditions are met, both relations become necessary, so no difference exists. The answer distinguishes that the agent alone can be independently necessitating in some cases, whereas the receiver alone cannot. Further discussion critiques the notion of independence and the possibility of a single thing being both agent and receiver. The passage concludes by noting that the possibility and impossibility are from different aspects (agency vs. reception), so no contradiction arises, though the commentator hints that this argument will be refuted later.

فعل
موجودات خارجية
إضافات
وجوب
إمكان
فاعل
قابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation of the agent to the effect?
A) Possibility
B) Necessity
C) Contingency
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Acceptance and action are not external existents because they have incompatible modal relations (necessity vs. possibility).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If acceptance is merely a mental relation, how do you ensure that your receptivity to the Qur'an goes beyond a mental acknowledgment and transforms your external actions? What specific change in your behavior would prove that your acceptance is real?
?
The author argues that your 'action' as a created being is only a mental consideration, not an external reality. When you claim to act for Allah, are you satisfied with the intention alone, or do you actually follow through with external deeds that match that intention? Where do you fall short, and what will you do to close that gap?
?
The agent's relation to its effect is necessity; the receiver's is possibility. In your relationship with Allah, do you see yourself as the passive receiver (possible) or as the agent (necessary)? Recognize that as a creature, you are only a receiver. Does this truth humble you when you plan your day? How will you consciously live as a receiver today?

Objection and Answer: Conditions of Existence
Objection and initial answer about conditions and independence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that when all conditions are met, both agent and receiver relations become necessary, and the initial answer that the agent alone can be independently necessitating.

Core Idea
When all conditions are present, both relations seem necessary—but the agent can be independent.

An objector challenges the previous argument by pointing out that if we consider all necessary conditions, both the agent and receiver relations become necessary, eliminating the distinction. The author responds by claiming that the agent alone may be independently necessitating in some cases. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the objection: 'When the receiver is taken alone, the existence of the received is not necessitated... But when both are taken with all the conditions for existence, their existence becomes necessary.' Understand the point: the distinction based on necessity/possibility collapses when all conditions are present.
The objector argues that the difference is only due to incomplete conditions, not inherent modality.
2
Study the Answer
The answer states: 'The agent alone may in some cases be independently necessitating.' This means the agent does not always require the receiver to bring about the effect, whereas the receiver always needs the agent.
3
Assess the Argument
Consider whether the answer fully resolves the objection. Does the possibility of an independent agent apply to all cases? The text leaves room for debate.
This is a critical moment in the overall argument.

Source Text

قلنا : ( وقد عرفت ) أن القبول والفعل بمعنى التأثير ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية. أيضاً (جوابه)، مع ( وأيضاً : فنسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان )، فلا يجتمعان واعترض على هذا بأن القابل إذا أخذ وحده لم يجب معه وجود المقبول، وكما أن الفاعل وحده لا يجب معه وجود المفعول، وإذا أخذا مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود المقبول والمفعول وجب وجودهما معهما، فلا فرق إذا بينهما في الوجوب والإمكان. وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده قد يكون في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجبا. قوله: ( وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده إلخ فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد أن المقبول إذا كان مما يجب أن يكون له محل ،قابل كما هو محل النزاع ففاعله قد يكون وحده في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجباً له، فهو ممنوع إذ لا بد له من القابل وإن أراد أن المقبول إذا لم يكن كذلك، ففاعله يجوز أن يكون مستقلاً في بعض الصور بإيجابه فهو مسلم ، لكن لا يلزم من هذا تناف في محل النزاع، إذ لا استقلال لشيء من القابل والفاعل بالإيجاب بالنسبة إلى المفعول والمقبول، ومن شرط التنافي أن يكون حصول المتنافيين بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد على أن في قوله، ولا يتصور في القابل شائبة مصادرة؛ لأن التصديق بهذا القول يتوقف على التصديق بأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً ، وإلا فقد يكون ذلك القابل هو الفاعل، فيكون القابل موجباً للمقبول وحده، فإن قلت : إيجابه ليس من حيث إنه ،قابل، بل من حيث إنه فاعل قلت : هذا إنما يفيد تغاير مفهومي القابل والفاعل، ولا يدل على أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون متصفاً بهذين المفهومين على ما هو، المدعى فتأمل هذا. وقد يدفع جواب الشارح أيضاً بأن إمكان الوجوب إنما هو من جهة الفاعلية، أيضاً . هذا المجيب، وامتناع الوجوب إنما هو من جهة القابلية، كما به صرح فإمكان الوجوب وامتناعه نيسا من جهة واحدة بل من جهتين مختلفتين هما الفاعلية والقابلية ولا محذور في ذلك . وستطلع في المقصد السادس على سقوط هذا الكلام بقي ها هنا شيء وهو كما صرح به

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you most strongly assume that if all conditions are met, the result is guaranteed? ?

Critique of the Answer: Independence and the Single Thing
Critique of the answer: independence and the problem of a single thing being both agent and receiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique of the previous answer, especially the issues of independence and whether a single thing can be both agent and receiver.

Core Idea
The agent's independence is debatable, and a single thing might be both agent and receiver.

The previous answer is now subjected to rigorous critique. The text examines whether the agent's independence holds in the context under discussion and whether a single entity can simultaneously be both agent and receiver, which would undermine the original argument. This lesson delves into the nuanced debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the Critique
The passage begins with 'His statement: 'And the answer that the agent alone etc. is debatable...' It questions the applicability of the answer. Follow the two scenarios presented.
2
Understand the Two Scenarios
The author considers two possibilities: (1) the received must have a place (receiver) – then the agent cannot be independent; (2) the received does not require a place – then the agent can be independent, but this does not affect the point of contention.
This shows the answer is either false or irrelevant.
3
Grasp the Circularity Charge
The next part accuses the answer of begging the question: it assumes that a single thing cannot be both agent and receiver, which is exactly what needs to be proven.
4
Consider the Conceptual Distinction
Even if agent and receiver are different concepts, the same entity could have both properties. The passage concludes that the answer only shows conceptual difference, not impossibility of coexistence.

قلنا : ( وقد عرفت ) أن القبول والفعل بمعنى التأثير ليسا من الموجودات الخارجية. أيضاً (جوابه)، مع ( وأيضاً : فنسبة الفاعل إلى المفعول بالوجوب ونسبة القابل إلى المقبول بالإمكان )، فلا يجتمعان واعترض على هذا بأن القابل إذا أخذ وحده لم يجب معه وجود المقبول، وكما أن الفاعل وحده لا يجب معه وجود المفعول، وإذا أخذا مع جميع ما يتوقف عليه وجود المقبول والمفعول وجب وجودهما معهما، فلا فرق إذا بينهما في الوجوب والإمكان. وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده قد يكون في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجبا. قوله: ( وأجيب بأن الفاعل وحده إلخ فيه بحث لأنه إن أراد أن المقبول إذا كان مما يجب أن يكون له محل ،قابل كما هو محل النزاع ففاعله قد يكون وحده في بعض الصور مستقلاً موجباً له، فهو ممنوع إذ لا بد له من القابل وإن أراد أن المقبول إذا لم يكن كذلك، ففاعله يجوز أن يكون مستقلاً في بعض الصور بإيجابه فهو مسلم ، لكن لا يلزم من هذا تناف في محل النزاع، إذ لا استقلال لشيء من القابل والفاعل بالإيجاب بالنسبة إلى المفعول والمقبول، ومن شرط التنافي أن يكون حصول المتنافيين بالنسبة إلى شيء واحد على أن في قوله، ولا يتصور في القابل شائبة مصادرة؛ لأن التصديق بهذا القول يتوقف على التصديق بأن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً ، وإلا فقد يكون ذلك القابل هو الفاعل، فيكون القابل موجباً للمقبول وحده، فإن قلت : إيجابه ليس من حيث إنه ،قابل، بل من حيث إنه فاعل قلت : هذا إنما يفيد تغاير مفهومي القابل والفاعل، ولا يدل على أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون متصفاً بهذين المفهومين على ما هو، المدعى فتأمل هذا. وقد يدفع جواب الشارح أيضاً بأن إمكان الوجوب إنما هو من جهة الفاعلية، أيضاً . هذا المجيب، وامتناع الوجوب إنما هو من جهة القابلية، كما به صرح فإمكان الوجوب وامتناعه نيسا من جهة واحدة بل من جهتين مختلفتين هما الفاعلية والقابلية ولا محذور في ذلك . وستطلع في المقصد السادس على سقوط هذا الكلام بقي ها هنا شيء وهو كما صرح به
We said: You have known that acceptance and action in the sense of effect are not among external existents. Also, the answer, and also: The relation of the agent to the effect is by necessity, and the relation of the receiver to the received is by possibility, so they do not coincide. An objection was raised against this: When the receiver is taken alone, the existence of the received is not necessitated, just as when the agent alone is taken, the existence of the effect is not necessitated. But when both are taken with all the conditions for existence, their existence becomes necessary. So there is no difference between them in necessity and possibility. The answer was given: The agent alone may in some cases be independently necessitating. His statement: 'And the answer that the agent alone etc. is debatable because if he means that the received, when it must have a place, is the point of contention, then its agent may be independently necessitating in some cases – this is denied because it requires a receiver. If he means that the received is not like that, then its agent may be independently necessitating in some cases – this is granted, but it does not entail conflict in the point of contention, because neither the receiver nor the agent independently necessitates with respect to the effect and the received. The condition of conflict is that the two opposing things occur with respect to a single thing. Moreover, his statement 'There is no consideration of a receiver' contains a begging of the question, because affirming this statement depends on affirming that a single thing cannot be both receiver and agent; otherwise, that receiver might be the agent, so the receiver necessitates the received alone. If you say: Its necessitation is not insofar as it is a receiver but insofar as it is an agent, I say: This only establishes the conceptual distinction between receiver and agent, but does not prove that a single thing cannot be described by both concepts, which is the claim. Consider this. The answer of the commentator may also be refuted by saying that the possibility of necessity is from the aspect of agency, and the impossibility of necessity is from the aspect of reception, as he explicitly stated. So possibility and impossibility are not from one aspect but from two different aspects: agency and reception, and there is no harm in that. You will see in the sixth objective the failure of this argument. There remains something here, which is as he stated.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents a key response to the earlier objection about the simple being. It begins by stating that acceptance and action (in the sense of effect) are not external existents but mental considerations (إضافات). Then it presents another argument: the relation of agent to effect is by necessity, while the relation of receiver to received is by possibility, so they cannot coexist. An objector argues that when all conditions are met, both relations become necessary, so no difference exists. The answer distinguishes that the agent alone can be independently necessitating in some cases, whereas the receiver alone cannot. Further discussion critiques the notion of independence and the possibility of a single thing being both agent and receiver. The passage concludes by noting that the possibility and impossibility are from different aspects (agency vs. reception), so no contradiction arises, though the commentator hints that this argument will be refuted later.

?

Resolution: Different Aspects of Possibility and Impossibility
Final refutation of the commentator's answer and hint at later invalidation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the final resolution that possibility of necessity and impossibility of necessity come from different aspects (agency vs. reception), so no contradiction occurs.

Core Idea
Possibility and impossibility are from different aspects, so no conflict arises.

The commentator's answer is further defended by noting that the possibility of necessity (from agency) and the impossibility of necessity (from reception) are not from the same aspect. Therefore, they do not contradict each other. This lesson presents that resolution and notes that the author will later show its failure.

Guided Path
1
Read the Refutation
Read: 'The answer of the commentator may also be refuted by saying that the possibility of necessity is from the aspect of agency, and the impossibility of necessity is from the aspect of reception.'
2
Understand the Two Aspects
The possibility of necessity (i.e., that the agent necessarily produces the effect) is from the agent's side. The impossibility of necessity (i.e., that the receiver cannot necessitate the received) is from the receiver's side. They are different aspects.
3
Recognize the Logical Point
Because the two modal claims are about different aspects (agency vs. reception), they do not conflict. There is no contradiction in saying that something is necessary from one perspective and not necessary from another.
4
Note the Final Remark
The author hints that this argument will be overturned later ('You will see in the sixth objective the failure of this argument'). This shows the ongoing nature of the debate.

The Simple Being: Agent and Receptacle from One Aspect?
Presents the objection that a simple being cannot be both agent and receptacle from one aspect, and gives the first answer and its rebuttal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical objection against a simple being being both agent and receptacle from one aspect, the attempted answer, and why it fails.

Core Idea
A simple being cannot be both agent and receptacle from one aspect without contradiction.

In the debate about divine attributes and actions, a key question arises: can a simple being (like God) be both the agent of an effect and the receptacle of that effect from the same aspect? This lesson presents the objection that this leads to a contradiction, followed by a classic answer and its rebuttal. You'll see how kalam scholars carefully analyze relationships to avoid logical inconsistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection states that if a simple being were both agent and receptacle from one aspect, then the effect would be both possible and impossible from that aspect. Try to understand why this is a contradiction.
Focus on the terms 'agent' (fa'il) and 'receptacle' (qabil).
2
Examine the first answer
The answer argues that a simple thing can have two different relations of necessity and possibility to another thing from two different aspects. Consider how this attempts to resolve the contradiction.
The answer introduces the idea of 'aspects' (jihaat) as distinct relational perspectives.
3
Understand the rebuttal
The rebuttal points out that the objection specifically assumes one aspect, while the answer assumes multiple aspects, so it misses the point. Notice how the rebuttal reasserts the original condition.
The debate hinges on the phrase 'from one aspect' (min jihatin waahidah).
4
Reflect on the incomplete second answer
The passage mentions that some scholars gave a different answer, but it is not fully presented. Consider why the text might leave this answer incomplete.
This may be a preview for later lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمفعوله، ولا يتصور ذلك في القابل؛ إذ لا بد من الفاعل فالفعل وحده موجب في الجملة والقبول وحده ليس بموجب أصلاً، فلو اجتمعا في شيء واحد من جهة واحدة لزم إمكان الوجوب، وامتناعه من تلك الجهة . ( والجواب : أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون للشيء البسيط إلى شيء آخر (نسبتان مختلفتان بالوجوب والإمكان ( من جهتين مختلفتين، فتجب ( النسبة الناشئة ) من جهة، ولا تجب ( النسبة الناشئة ( من جهة ) أخرى، ورد هذا الجواب : بأن كلامنا في أن البسيط لا يكون قابلاً وفاعلاً من جهة واحدة، وعلى ما ذكرتم تكون تلك الجهة متعددة ) ومنهم من أجاب ) : عن الوجه
To its effect, and that cannot be conceived in the receptacle; because the agent is necessary, so the act alone is necessitating in the sentence, and reception alone is not necessitating at all. So if they were combined in one thing from one aspect, then possibility of existence and impossibility from that aspect would be necessary. (The answer: It is not impossible for the simple thing to have two different relations of necessity and possibility to another thing from two different aspects, so the relation arising from one aspect is necessary, and the relation arising from another aspect is not necessary. And this answer was rejected: because our discussion is about the simple not being a receptacle and an agent from one aspect, and according to what you mentioned, that aspect would be multiple) And among them is one who answered: concerning the aspect.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and an answer in the debate about the simple being (al-basit al-haqiqi). The objection argues that if a simple being were both agent (fa'il) and receptacle (qabil) from one aspect, then the effect would be both possible and impossible from that aspect, a contradiction. The first answer claims that the simple can have two different relations (nisbatayn) of necessity and possibility from two different aspects, so no contradiction. The rebuttal points out that the objection specifically assumes one aspect, and the answer assumes multiple aspects, which does not meet the objection. The passage also mentions another answer from some scholars, but does not elaborate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the first answer successfully refutes the objection by showing a simple being can have multiple relations.
Correction: The rebuttal shows that the answer changes the subject: the objection assumes a single aspect, but the answer assumes multiple aspects, so it does not address the original contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the contradiction that the objection claims would arise if a simple being were both agent and receptacle from one aspect?
A) The effect would be both necessary and impossible from that aspect.
B) The agent would be unnecessary.
C) The receptacle would be inefficient.
D) The simple would become composite.

Key Takeaway
The attempt to reconcile a simple being having both agency and receptivity from one aspect leads to a contradiction, and the common answer fails by shifting to multiple aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a moment in your life when you tried to be both the one in control (agent) and the one who is passive (receptacle) regarding the same outcome—like wanting to earn someone's love while also demanding it. Did you feel an internal contradiction? How does this lesson's logical impossibility mirror the spiritual tension you experienced? What will you do to align your heart with one role before God?

When you read the objection that a simple being cannot be both agent and receptacle from one aspect, do you see a reflection of your own attempts to serve both God and your ego in the same act? For example, giving charity while hoping for recognition. In that moment, your intention becomes a 'simple being' trying to combine two contradictory relations. Will you repent from that mixed intention and purify your act for God alone?

The Second Answer: General Possibility of the Receptacle
States and critiques the second answer (general possibility).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second answer (general possibility) fails to resolve the problem of the simple being and leads back to the first answer.

Core Idea
General possibility includes specific possibility, so it fails to solve the problem.

The second answer attempts to resolve the issue by claiming that the receptacle's relation is by general possibility, which does not contradict necessity. However, this distinction leads to an objection: general possibility includes specific possibility, creating a contradiction when compared with the agent's necessary relation. The only way out is to return to the first answer's two-relations approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument: the second answer, the objection, and the resolution.
Notice the technical terms: الإمكان العام (general possibility) and الإمكان الخاص (specific possibility).
2
Identify the second answer's claim
The second answer says the receptacle's relation is by general possibility, which does not contradict necessity. Understand what general possibility means here.
General possibility is a broader category that includes both necessary and possible relations.
3
Follow the objection
The objection points out that general possibility includes specific possibility, so it cannot exclude it. This leads to a contradiction when the agent's relation is considered.
The agent's relation is by necessity (وجوب), which does not admit specific possibility.
4
See the resolution
The passage concludes that to avoid contradiction, one must revert to the first answer involving two different relations from two aspects, rendering the second answer null.
This shows that the second answer is not a standalone solution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني ( بأن نسبة القابل) إلى المقبول (بالإمكان العام وهو لا ينافي الوجوب )، بل يجامعه، لا بالإمكان الخاص الذي ينافيه . وأورد عليه أنه ( أي انتساب القابل إلى المقبول ) بالإمكان العام المحتمل للإمكان الخاص، ولذلك لا يمكن عدم القبول من حيث أنه مقبول مع وجود القابل ( ويتم الدليل حينئذ ؛ إذ نقول : نسبة الفاعل أن تكون بالوجوب، ونسبة القابل لا يتعين أن تكون كذلك ) ، أو نقول بعبارة يتعين أخرى : نسبة الفاعل لا تحتمل الإمكان الخاص ونسبة القابل تحتمله، فيلزم أن تكون نسبة واحدة محتملة للإمكان الخاص غير محتملة له إلا أن يعاد إلى الجواب الأول) فيقال: جاز أن يكون هناك نسبتان من جهتين إحداهما واجبة على التعيين، غير محتملة للإمكان الخاص والأخرى محتملة له فيكون) الجواب (الثاني لغوا)
The second (that the relation of the receptacle) to the received (is by general possibility, and it does not contradict necessity), rather it coexists with it, not by specific possibility which contradicts it. And it was objected to it that (the attribution of the receptacle to the received) is by general possibility which is inclusive of specific possibility, and therefore it is impossible for the reception to be absent from it inasmuch as it is received with the existence of the receptacle (and the proof is then completed; for we say: the relation of the agent is by necessity, and the relation of the receptacle is not determined to be so), or we say in another expression: the relation of the agent does not admit specific possibility and the relation of the receptacle admits it, so it necessitates that a single relation is inclusive of specific possibility and not inclusive of it, unless it returns to the first answer) so it is said: it is permissible that there are two relations from two aspects, one of them necessarily determined, not inclusive of specific possibility, and the other inclusive of it, so the second answer (is null).
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage presents the second answer to the problem of the simple being. The answer claims that the receptacle's relation to what it receives is by general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), which does not contradict necessity, unlike specific possibility. The objection argues that this general possibility includes specific possibility, so the receptacle's relation would still admit specific possibility, leading to a contradiction when compared to the agent's necessary relation. To avoid contradiction, one must return to the first answer involving two different relations from two aspects, rendering the second answer null.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
الوجوب
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that general possibility solves the problem because it does not contradict necessity.
Correction: General possibility includes specific possibility, which does contradict necessity, so the second answer fails unless supplemented by the first answer's two-relations view.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why does the second answer fail?
A) Because general possibility excludes specific possibility.
B) Because general possibility includes specific possibility, causing a contradiction.
C) Because the receptacle's relation is always necessary.
D) Because the agent's relation is not necessary.

Key Takeaway
The second answer fails because general possibility includes specific possibility, so the only consistent solution is the first answer's two-relations approach.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you tend to picture them as separate ‘parts’ added to His essence? This lesson shows how easily we can fall into contradiction. Do you truly affirm that God is simple, while also saying He knows, wills, and acts? How can you train your heart to hold both truths without compromise?
?
The second answer seemed neat and plausible, yet it was shown to be insufficient. Have you ever settled for a comfortable explanation of a matter of faith, only to later realize it was shallow? Are you willing to follow the arguments—even if they challenge your initial understanding—in order to reach the truth about your Creator?
?
This passage forces us to think carefully about relations and categories. But truth about God is not merely academic; it transforms worship. Do you recognize that how you conceive of Allah's attributes affects your love, fear, and trust in Him? Will you commit to seeking the sound understanding, even when it requires mental effort?

The Philosophers' Statement on Bodily Power
Philosophers define bodily power as a state in the body that does not yield infinite effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' definition of bodily power and why it cannot produce infinite effects, distinguishing between agency and receptivity.

Core Idea
Bodily power is a state in the body, not a source of infinite effect.

This passage introduces the philosophers' claim about bodily power (al-quwwa al-jismaniyya). They define it as a state in the body and argue that it does not produce an infinite effect. The commentary clarifies the distinction between the agent's necessary relation and the receptacle's dependent relation, and notes that abstract souls can produce infinite motions. Let's examine the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of bodily power and the claim that it does not produce an infinite effect.
2
Understand the agent-receptacle distinction
The commentary explains that the agent's relation is necessary because it is independent, while the receptacle's relation is not necessary because it depends on the agent.
This is key to understanding why bodily power is limited.
3
Clarify the phrase 'state in the body'
The passage distinguishes between a state in the body and a state related to the body. Celestial souls are related to bodies but are not states in bodies, allowing them infinite power.
4
Review the exception
Note that abstract celestial souls are an exception: they can produce infinite motions while being related to bodies.
This reinforces that bodily power is strictly a state in the body.
