Objection and Theological Clarification on Infinite Intensity
Objection, response, and clarification on infinite intensity vs. duration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the philosophers' argument, the response by some verifiers, and the theological distinction between infinite intensity and infinite duration.

Core Idea
The objection denies actual divisibility; theologians separate intensity from duration.

In this lesson, we explore a rebuttal to the philosophers' argument. An objector claims that dividing the time does not prove actual possibility of a faster motion. The passage also clarifies that theologians accept infinite duration (e.g., eternal bliss and punishment) but reject infinite intensity in the sense of requiring actual infinite divisions. Additionally, a response from some verifiers and its critique are presented.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage from the objection onwards. Notice how the objector denies the actual possibility of traversing half the time.
The objector says 'we do not accept that traversing that distance in half that time is possible in reality'.
2
Understand the theological clarification
The text explains that theologians permit infinite duration (like eternal life) but not infinite intensity as requiring actually infinite divisions. Grasp this distinction.
The example of paradise and hellfire is used.
3
Examine the response and its critique
Some verifiers attempt to answer the objection by defining infinite intensity as that which the intellect cannot conceive as surpassable. But the response is criticized because actual conceivability is denied.
The passage ends with a critique of the response.

Misconception & Correction

The Philosophers' Proof Against Infinite Physical Forces
Philosophers use ratio of halves to deny infinite physical force.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that physical forces cannot produce infinite effects in duration or number, using the ratio of halves.

Core Idea
If a whole had infinite force, half would too—an impossible contradiction.

This lesson presents the core dispute between theologians and philosophers over whether physical forces can act infinitely. The philosophers offer a simple yet powerful proof based on the ratio of forces between half and whole bodies. Understanding this proof is essential for grasping later arguments about the soul and eternal punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the philosophers state their position and then provide a proof.
The proof uses the ratio of halves to show impossibility.
2
Identify the two sides of the dispute
The passage first mentions that theologians (Ash'aris/Mu'tazila) allow infinite physical force. Then the philosophers deny it. Mark these opposing views.
The reason for the theologians' view is the permanence of Paradise and Hell.
3
Follow the ratio argument
The philosophers argue that the force of half a body is half that of the whole because both are equal in receptivity. If the whole had infinite force, the half would also have infinite force (half of infinity?), leading to absurdity.
The argument assumes force is proportional to size due to common corporeality.
4
Reflect on why this matters
This proof is a premise for proving that the souls of the spheres are abstract and can produce perpetual motion. It also impacts debates about eternal punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما اللاتناهي في المدة والعدة فقد جوزه المتكلمون الأشاعرة القائلون باستناد )

دائمان، ولا يتصور ذلك إلا بدوام الأبدان ،وقواها فتكون تلك القوى مؤثرة في الأبدان تأثيراً غير متناه زماناً ،وعدداً، ومنعه الحكماء وقالوا : يمتنع لا تناهي القوى الجسمانية في المدة والعدة في الحركة الطبيعية والقسرية ) واحتجوا عليه ) أي على انتفاء اللاتناهي وامتناعه فيهما بأن قوة (النصف أي نصف الجسم (في) التحريك الطبيعي نصف قوة الكل في ذلك التحريك وإنما قلنا : إن النسبة بين قوتي النصف والكل بالنصفية ( لتساوي) الجسم (الصغير) الذي هو (الصغير) الذي هو النصف، (و) الجسم (الكبير) الذي هو الكل. ( في القبول ) أي قبول الحركة؛ ( لأنه) أي لأن ذلك القبول للجسمية المشتركة بينهما ( وتفاوتهما ) أي ولتفاوت الصغير

قوله : (غير متناه زماناً وعدداً بمعنى أنه لا يقف عند حد، وهو المراد بقولهم القوة الجسمانية لا تقوى على أثر غير متناه في المدة والعدة لأنه مقدمة لإثبات النفوس المجردة للأفلاك، لأن نفوسها المنطبقة لا تقوى أن تفعل حركات لا تنقطع، فما قيل : إن اللازم من دوام النعيم والعذاب هو اللاتناهي بمعنى لا يقف والكلام في الغير المتناهي الذي كان الواقع غير متناه ،سهو، ثم أما تجويزهم ذلك على عدم تجرد النفس الناطقة وأنها هي الهيكل المحسوس، وأن البدن مع قواها باقية ليكون المعذب والمنعم هو فاعل الحسنات والسيئات وأن المراد بقوله تعالى كلما نضجت جلودهم بدلناهم جلوداً غيرها ﴾ [النساء: ٥٦] تبديل التركيب والهيئة على ما في تفسير القاضي.

مبني

قوله : ( في الحركة الطبيعية والقسرية تخصيص الحركة بالذكر للاهتمام بشأنها وإلا فالدليل يجري في كل أثر غير متناه في المدة والعدة، فلا يرد أن الدليل أخص من الدعوى. قوله : ( على انتفاء اللاتناهي يعني أن الضمير المجرور راجع إلى النفي المستفاد من

قوله : لا يفيد والمراد بالانتفاء الامتناع . قوله : ( فيهما ) أي في المدة والعدة.

قوله : ( أن قوة النصف إلخ) أي النسبة بين القوتين كالنسبة بين الجسمين على ما يدل عليه قوله : والفاعلان متفاوتان بحسب تفاوت المحل فذكر النصف للتصوير.

جميع

الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء لا يثبتون للقوى الجسمانية تأثيراً، كما سيأتي في الجواب، فكأن المراد بالمتكلمين المجوزين لعدم تناهي تأثير القوة الجسمانية في المدة والعدة، بناء على أن نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار دائمان هو المعتزلة، ويحتمل أن يكون إطلاق التأثير على سبيل المجاز، فإن الأشاعرة قد يطلقون المؤثر والعلة على غيره تعالى مجازاً بحسب الترتب الظاهري أي على سبيل جري ،العادة، فحاصل النزاع أنا نجوز عدم تناهي الترتب الظاهري بين القوى الجسمانية والآثار، بناء على أن المؤثر هو الله تعالى، والفلاسفة لا يجوزونه؛

لأن المؤثر عندهم هو القوى . والقول بأن المراد التأثير ولو بطريق الكسب والمباشرة أبعد .
His statement: (As for the infinite in duration and number, the theologians — the Ash'aris who say that [all things are based on God] — have allowed it)

[They say that bliss and punishment] are everlasting, and that cannot be conceived except by the permanence of the bodies and their forces; so those forces would be effective in the bodies with an effect infinite in time and number. But the philosophers denied this and said: It is impossible for physical forces to be infinite in duration and number in natural and coercive motion. And they argued for it — i.e., for the negation and impossibility of infinity in them — that the force of the half, i.e., half of the body, in natural motion is half the force of the whole in that motion. Indeed we said that the ratio between the forces of the half and the whole is half because of the equality of the small body — i.e., the half — and the large body — i.e., the whole — in receptivity — i.e., the receptivity of motion — because that receptivity is due to the common corporeality between them, and their difference — i.e., the difference of the small and the large is in [size].

His statement: (Infinite in time and number, meaning that it does not stop at a limit, and that is what they mean by saying 'the physical force cannot produce an effect infinite in duration and number' because it is a premise for proving the abstract souls of the spheres, because their applied souls cannot perform ceaseless motions. So what is said — that the necessary consequence of the permanence of bliss and punishment is the infinite in the sense of not stopping, while the discussion is about the infinite whose actual occurrence is infinite — is a mistake. Then, their allowing that is based on the non-abstractness of the rational soul and that it is the sensible body, and that the body with its forces remains so that the punished and blessed one is the doer of good and bad deeds, and that the meaning of His statement 'Whenever their skins are roasted We will replace them with other skins' [Quran 4:56] is the change of composition and form, according to what is in the commentary of al-Qadi.

It is based on that.

His statement: (In natural and coercive motion) The specification of motion by mention is for emphasis on its matter; otherwise, the proof applies to every effect infinite in duration and number, so it is not said that the proof is more specific than the claim. His statement: (On the negation of infinity) means that the enclosed pronoun refers to the negation understood from his statement 'does not benefit', and what is meant by 'negation' is impossibility. His statement: (In them both) i.e., in duration and number.

His statement: (That the force of the half, etc.) i.e., the ratio between the two forces is like the ratio between the two bodies, as indicated by his statement: 'The two agents differ according to the difference of the locus.' So mentioning the half is for illustration.

All possible things are from God Almighty initially; they do not affirm effect for physical forces, as will come in the answer. So it appears that what is meant by 'the theologians who allow the non-finite effect of physical force in duration and number' — based on the fact that the bliss of Paradise and the punishment of Hell are everlasting — is the Mu'tazila. And it is possible that the attribution of effect is metaphorical, for the Ash'aris may use 'effect' and 'cause' for other than Him metaphorically, according to apparent order, i.e., by way of habitual course. So the summary of the dispute is: We allow the non-finiteness of the apparent order between physical forces and effects, based on the fact that the true agent is God, and the philosophers do not allow it because the agent according to them is the forces. And the statement that what is meant is effect even by way of acquisition and direct action is more remote.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage addresses the philosophers' argument that physical forces cannot produce effects infinite in duration (time) or number (count). The theologians (here identified as Ash'aris, but later clarified as Mu'tazila) permit such infinity based on the everlastingness of Paradise and Hell, requiring permanent bodies and forces. The philosophers object and provide a proof: the force of half a body is half the force of the whole body, because the bodies are equal in receptivity (due to shared corporeality) and differ only in size. If a whole body could produce an infinite effect, the half would also have to produce an infinite effect proportional to its size, which is impossible because an infinite effect from a smaller force would lead to contradiction. The commentator explains the meaning of 'infinite in duration and number' as 'not stopping at a limit', and notes that this argument is a premise for proving the existence of abstract souls for the spheres, which can produce perpetual motions. He then discusses the theological context: the Mu'tazila, who affirm human agency and eternal punishment, allow infinite effects for physical forces, while Ash'aris do not because they attribute all effects to God, using the term 'effect' metaphorically for the apparent habitual order. The passage ends by clarifying that the proof applies to all effects, not just motion, and that the ratio of forces mirrors the ratio of bodies.

Key Terms
اللاتناهي في المدة والعدة
القوة الجسمانية
النسبة بين القوتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the philosophers' claim that the force of half a body is half that of the whole?
A) The half body has less mass, so less force naturally.
B) Both bodies are equal in receptivity due to shared corporeality, differing only in size.
C) The philosophers assume all bodies have the same density.
D) The Quran states that smaller bodies have weaker forces.

Key Takeaway
Physical forces cannot be infinite in duration or number because force is proportional to body size, making infinite force contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that your own physical abilities are unlimited? How does this philosophical proof humble you regarding the limits of your own body and the Creator behind it?
?
If even the forces of bodies are finite, what does that tell you about trusting in your own strength versus trusting in the One who created those bodies and set their limits?
?
Can you recall a time when you assumed something in your life could continue forever? How does this argument change your perspective on the temporality of all physical things and the need to turn to the Eternal?

Meaning and Implications of Infinite Duration and Number
Clarifies the meaning of 'infinite' and its role in proving abstract souls and the theological context.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'infinite in duration and number' as 'not stopping at a limit', its role in proving abstract souls, and the different theological views on physical causality.

Core Idea
Infinite here means endless, not unlimited intensity.

The previous lesson presented the philosophers' proof. This lesson clarifies what 'infinite in duration and number' actually means and why it matters for proving that the souls of the spheres are abstract. It also explores how Ash'aris and Mu'tazila interpret the causality of physical forces in light of eternal punishment.

Guided Path
1
Examine the definition of infinite
The passage explains that 'infinite in duration and number' means 'does not stop at a limit'. Contrast this with other meanings of infinity.
The term is used in the context of endless acts, not intensity.
2
Understand the purpose of the proof
The proof is a premise for establishing that the souls of the spheres are abstract, because physical forces cannot produce endless motion.
This connects to the broader philosophical system.
3
Analyze the theological debate
The commentator discusses that the theologians who allow infinite physical force are the Mu'tazila (who affirm human agency), while Ash'aris use 'effect' metaphorically because all true agency belongs to God.
This explains the earlier mention of 'Ash'aris' as a possible misidentification.
4
Resolve a common misunderstanding
Some might think that the infinite nature of punishment in Paradise/Hell implies infinite physical force, but the commentator clarifies that the discussion is about the actual occurrence of infinite effects, not potential infinity.

قوله : ( وأما اللاتناهي في المدة والعدة فقد جوزه المتكلمون الأشاعرة القائلون باستناد )

Misconception & Correction

Causality and Created Things

The Philosophers' Denial of Infinite Corporeal Powers: First Premise (Natural Motion)
Al-Jurjani states the philosophers' denial of infinite corporeal powers and provides the first proof from natural motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise of the philosophers' proof that corporeal powers cannot be infinite, based on the proportional relationship between power and body in natural motion.

Core Idea
Corporeal powers are proportional to their subjects; an infinite power would require an infinite body.

The philosophers argue that corporeal powers cannot produce infinite effects because they are inherently limited by the size of their subject. This lesson presents their first premise, which draws on the example of natural motion: the power of half a body is exactly half the power of the whole. Understanding this premise is essential for grasping the larger debate about the eternity of reward and punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: a claim, a denial, and a proof.
2
Identify the claim and the proof structure
The passage first presents the philosophers' denial of infinite corporeal powers, then gives a proof. Locate where the proof begins and how it is structured.
The proof starts with 'واحتجوا عليه'.
3
Understand the reasoning: equal receptivity, proportional power
Grasp why the power of half is exactly half the power of the whole. The key is that both bodies are equal in receptivity (due to common corporeality), but powers differ because power divides with the division of the subject.
The ratio of powers is the same as the ratio of the bodies' sizes.
4
Consider the implication for infinite corporeal powers
If an effect were infinite, the power producing it would have to be infinite, which would require an infinite body. Since bodies are finite, infinite corporeal powers are impossible.
This is the core of the philosophers' refutation.
5
Reflect on the broader context
Think about how this premise connects to the larger debate about the eternity of reward and punishment and the role of corporeal powers.
The theologians (Mu'tazilites) permit infinite effects because they see God as the true agent, unlike the philosophers who attribute agency to the powers themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الخامس : قول الحكماء في القوة الجسمانية

قوله : ( في الحركة الطبيعية والقسرية تخصيص الحركة بالذكر للاهتمام بشأنها وإلا فالدليل يجري في كل أثر غير متناه في المدة والعدة، فلا يرد أن الدليل أخص من الدعوى. قوله : ( على انتفاء اللاتناهي يعني أن الضمير المجرور راجع إلى النفي المستفاد من
قوله : لا يفيد والمراد بالانتفاء الامتناع . قوله : ( فيهما ) أي في المدة والعدة.
قوله : ( أن قوة النصف إلخ) أي النسبة بين القوتين كالنسبة بين الجسمين على ما يدل عليه قوله : والفاعلان متفاوتان بحسب تفاوت المحل فذكر النصف للتصوير.
جميع
الممكنات إلى الله تعالى ابتداء لا يثبتون للقوى الجسمانية تأثيراً، كما سيأتي في الجواب، فكأن المراد بالمتكلمين المجوزين لعدم تناهي تأثير القوة الجسمانية في المدة والعدة، بناء على أن نعيم أهل الجنة وعذاب أهل النار دائمان هو المعتزلة، ويحتمل أن يكون إطلاق التأثير على سبيل المجاز، فإن الأشاعرة قد يطلقون المؤثر والعلة على غيره تعالى مجازاً بحسب الترتب الظاهري أي على سبيل جري ،العادة، فحاصل النزاع أنا نجوز عدم تناهي الترتب الظاهري بين القوى الجسمانية والآثار، بناء على أن المؤثر هو الله تعالى، والفلاسفة لا يجوزونه؛
لأن المؤثر عندهم هو القوى . والقول بأن المراد التأثير ولو بطريق الكسب والمباشرة أبعد .
والكبير ( في القوة فإنها أي القوة تنقسم بانقسام المحل)، فالقابلان أعني الجسمين الصغير والكبير متساويان في قبول الحركة الطبيعية، لا تفاوت من جهتهما أصلاً. والفاعلان للتحريك الطبيعي أعني القوتين متفاوتان بحسب تفاوت المحل، ولما كان تفاوت المحلين بالنصفية كان تفاوت القوتين بالنصفية أيضاً، فيكون التفاوت بين أثريهما أيضاً كذلك؛ إذ لا تفاوت في الأثر هاهنا إلا باعتبار تفاوت المؤثرين
قوله : (تنقسم بانقسام المحل لكونها سارية في جملته وإلا لكانت قوة البعض دون الكل. قوله : (إذ لا تفاوت في الأثر إلخ) أي بالنظر إلى نفس الجسمين، وأما التفاوت باعتبار الأمور الخارجة عنهما فلا يضر ؛ لأنا نفرض عدم التفاوت بينهما في تلك الأمور، فاندفع ما قيل: إن الحركة في الخلاء محال فلا بد من ملأ يقع فيه الحركتان ولا شك أن ممانعة الجسم الكبير بسبب كبر حجمه أكثر من ممانعة الجسم الصغير حينئذ لم يكن التفاوت بين الحركتين على نسبة تفاوت المتحركين فيجوز أن تكون الحركتان كلتاهما غير متناهيتين، وإن كانت القوتان متفاوتتين بحسب تفاوت الجسمين، وذلك لأنا نفرض عدم التفاوت بحسب الملأ بأن يكون معاوقة الملأ الذي وقع فيه حركة النصف مثل معاوقة الملأ الذي وقع فيه حركة الكل باختلاف الملاين في الرقة والغلظ .
The Fifth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: The Philosophers' Statement on Corporeal Power. They are eternal, and that is not conceivable except with the perpetuity of bodies and their powers, so those powers are effective in bodies with an effect infinite in time and number. The philosophers denied this and said: The non-finiteness of corporeal powers in duration and number in natural and violent motion is impossible. And they argued for that, i.e., for the negation of non-finiteness and its impossibility in both, by saying: The power of the half, i.e., half of the body, in natural motion is half the power of the whole in that motion. We only say that the ratio between the powers of the half and the whole is by halfness because the small body, which is the half, and the large body, which is the whole, are equal in receptivity, i.e., the reception of motion, because that reception is due to the common corporeality between them, and their difference, i.e., the difference of the small and the large, is in power, for power divides with the division of the subject. So the two recipients, namely the small and the large bodies, are equal in receiving natural motion, with no difference from their side at all. The two agents for natural motion, namely the two powers, differ according to the difference of the subject, and since the difference of the two subjects was by halfness, the difference of the two powers was also by halfness, so the difference between their two effects is likewise, because there is no difference in the effect here except by consideration of the difference of the agents. His saying: (In natural and violent motion) is the specification of motion for the sake of concern for it, otherwise the proof applies to every effect infinite in duration and number, so it is not objected that the proof is more specific than the claim. His saying: (On the negation of non-finiteness) means that the attached pronoun refers to the negation derived from his saying: it does not benefit, and by negation is meant impossibility. His saying: (in both) means in duration and number. His saying: (that the power of the half, etc.) means the ratio between the two powers is like the ratio between the two bodies, as indicated by his saying: the two agents differ according to the difference of the subject, so he mentioned the half for illustration. All contingents are from God Most High initially; they do not posit effect for corporeal powers, as will come in the answer. So it is as if by the theologians who permit the non-finiteness of the effect of corporeal power in duration and number, based on the fact that the bliss of the people of Paradise and the torment of the people of Hell are eternal, it is the Mu'tazilites. And it is possible that the attribution of effect is metaphorical, for the Ash'arites may attribute the effect and cause to other than Him metaphorically according to apparent order, i.e., by way of habit. So the gist of the dispute is that we permit the non-finiteness of apparent order between corporeal powers and effects, based on the fact that the effective agent is God, and the philosophers do not permit it, because for them the effective agent is the powers. And the statement that by effect is meant even by way of acquisition and direct action is more remote. And the large (in power, for it, i.e., power, divides with the division of the subject), so the two recipients, namely the small and large bodies, are equal in receiving natural motion, with no difference from their side at all. The two agents for natural motion, namely the two powers, differ according to the difference of the subject, and since the difference of the two subjects was by halfness, the difference of the two powers was also by halfness, so the difference between their two effects is likewise; for there is no difference in the effect here except by consideration of the difference of the agents. His saying: (it divides with the division of the subject) because it pervades its entirety, otherwise the power of some would be less than the whole. His saying: (for there is no difference in the effect, etc.) means with respect to the bodies themselves; as for difference due to external matters, it does not harm, for we assume no difference between them in those matters. So what is said is refuted: that motion in a void is impossible, so there must be a medium in which the two motions occur, and without doubt the resistance of the large body due to its size is greater than the resistance of the small body; then the difference between the two motions would not be in proportion to the difference of the movers, so it is possible that both motions are infinite, even if the two powers differ according to the difference of the two bodies. That is because we assume no difference regarding the medium, such that the resistance of the medium in which the motion of the half occurs is like the resistance of the medium in which the motion of the whole occurs, by the difference of the media in rarefaction and density.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the philosophers' position that corporeal powers cannot be infinite in duration and number. It begins by presenting the background: the eternity of reward and punishment implies the perpetuity of bodies and their powers, producing infinite effects. The philosophers deny this impossibility. The core of the lesson is the first premise of their proof, which concerns natural motion. The argument states that the power of half a body in natural motion is exactly half the power of the whole body. This is because the two bodies (the half and the whole) are equal in their receptivity to motion, since that receptivity arises from their common corporeality. However, the powers differ in proportion to the size of the subject, because power divides with the division of the subject. Since the subjects differ by the ratio of half, the powers also differ by the ratio of half, and consequently the effects also differ by that same ratio. The commentary explains key points: the specification of motion is for emphasis, but the proof applies to any effect; the term 'non-finite' means it does not stop at a limit; and the intended theologians who permit infinite corporeal power are the Mu'tazilites, while the Ash'arites metaphorically attribute effects to powers while affirming God as the true agent. The passage also responds to an objection regarding the medium of motion, showing that even if the medium resists differently, we can assume equality in external conditions. The proof establishes that since powers and effects are proportional, an infinite power would require an infinite subject, which is impossible for corporeal powers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just studied a proof that every corporeal power is limited by the size of its subject. In your daily life, you rely on countless finite powers—your strength, your intellect, your lifespan. How does this realization make you feel about your own limitations? Are you humbled, frustrated, or motivated? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in the One whose power is infinite?
?
The philosophers argued that only a non-corporeal being can have infinite power. If you truly believe that Allah’s power is unlimited, do you live as if your own finite powers are enough, or do you constantly turn to Him for what you cannot do? When was the last time you admitted, 'I cannot do this on my own'? If you cannot recall, what does that say about your awareness of your limits?
?
This lesson shows that effects are proportional to powers, and powers to subjects. If your actions (effects) seem small or weak, perhaps it is because your 'subject'—your effort, sincerity, or reliance on Allah—is limited. Are you satisfied with producing only finite effects in your worship and character? What will you change today to increase your 'power' by expanding your subject—through more knowledge, stronger faith, or deeper reliance on Allah?

The Second Premise: Violent Motion and the Ratio of Power
Al-Jurjani clarifies the ratio of power in violent motion and dismisses a doubt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise of the philosophers' proof from violent motion, including the ratio of power between bodies, the key assumptions, and how the objection of Abu al-Barakat is refuted.

Core Idea
In violent motion, a larger body has proportionally less effective power due to greater natural resistance.

This lesson unpacks the second premise of the philosophers' argument for the finitude of corporeal powers. Al-Jurjani clarifies the precise ratio between the power of a half-sized body and a double-sized body in receiving violent motion. He also explains the assumptions that block an objection by Abu al-Barakat, reinforcing the overall conclusion that infinite corporeal powers are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meaning of 'power of the half'
Read the first line of the passage. The term 'power of the half' is defined as 'half of the half'—it refers to the body that is assumed to have double weakness. Grasp this technical definition before proceeding.
Do not confuse 'half of the half' with half of the body's size; it means half of the resistance.
2
Identify the ratio and its basis
The passage states that the double body has half the power of the half. Note that this ratio arises because natural resistance (the obstacle to forced motion) is twice as great in the double body when external conditions are equal.
The ratio is based on the assumption of no difference in external matters and that motion does not require a specific time.
3
See how the objection of Abu al-Barakat is dismissed
The commentary explicitly says that the doubt of Abu al-Barakat does not apply here because of the stated assumptions. Understand that this doubt would only be relevant if motion required a fixed time, which is denied.
The refutation will be fully explained later in the context of the impossibility of a void.
4
Connect to the overall proof
Realize that this premise, together with the first, shows that corporeal powers are finite and proportional. This leads to the conclusion that infinite corporeal powers cannot exist.
This completes the philosophers' proof against infinite duration and number of effects from corporeal powers.

قوله : (قوة النصف أي نصف النصف وهو الجسم المفروض ضعفه. قوله : ( بحسب زيادة الضعف إلخ ) بناء على فرض عدم التفاوت في الأمور الخارجة عنهما، وعلى أن ماهية الحركة لا تقتضي قدراً معيناً من الزمان على ما سيجيء في بيان امتناع الخلاء، فلا يرد شبهة أبي البركات هاهنا . قوله : ( كان نسبة القبول إلخ) أي بالنسبة إلى ذات الجسمين؛ لأنا فرضنا التساوي بينهما الأمور الخارجة عنهما . قوله : ( نصف قوة النصف أي نصف الضعف لا نصف الجسم كما يتبادر إلى الوهم .
His saying: (the power of the half means half of the half, and it is the body assumed to be double its weakness). His saying: (according to the increase of weakness, etc.) based on the assumption of no difference in external matters, and that the essence of motion does not necessitate a specific amount of time as will come in the explanation of the impossibility of a void, so the doubt of Abu al-Barakat does not apply here. His saying: (the ratio of acceptance, etc.) means with respect to the essence of the two bodies, for we assumed equality between them in external matters. His saying: (half the power of the half means half of the weakness, not half of the body as might initially occur to the mind.)
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage covers the second premise of the philosophers' proof, which concerns violent motion, and includes commentary that reinforces the conclusion. The second premise states that the power of the weak (i.e., double of a body) in receiving violent motion is half the power of the half. The commentary clarifies that 'the power of the half' here refers to half of the half (i.e., a quarter of the original?), more precisely it means half of the weakness, not half of the body. This is because in violent motion, the agent is assumed to be the same, but the resistance (the natural inclination opposing motion) is greater in the larger body. Since the resistance in the double body is twice that of the half, the power of the double is half that of the half. The commentary also addresses an objection by Abu al-Barakat, stating that it does not apply because we assume no difference in external conditions and because motion does not require a specific time. The overall conclusion is that since both premises show that corporeal powers and their effects are finite and proportional, infinite corporeal powers are impossible. This completes the philosophers' proof against infinite duration and number of effects from corporeal powers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'half the power of the half' means half of the half body's power (i.e., quarter of the original).
Correction: It actually means half of the weakness (resistance), i.e., half of the natural opposition to forced motion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The second premise shows that corporeal powers in violent motion are proportional to natural resistance, setting an upper bound that supports the impossibility of infinite power.

The First Premise: Bodily Power Divides with Its Substrate
States the first premise about bodily power dividing with its substrate, with lemma, clarification, objection, and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first premise that bodily power divides proportionally with its substrate and how the argument handles potential objections about external factors like medium resistance.

Core Idea
Bodily power scales with body size; effects follow the same ratio.

This passage lays the foundation for a key argument about natural motion. Before we can analyze why objects move differently, we must grasp that the power moving a body is not independent—it is spread throughout the body and proportional to its size. The author sets up a clear proportionality and then defends it against an objection involving the medium.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise and lemma
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the core claim: bodily power divides with the division of its substrate. The lemma compares two bodies, small and large, and states their powers differ by the same ratio as their sizes.
Pay attention to the terms: القوة (power), المحل (substrate), النصفية (halfness).
2
Understand the equality of receptivity
The author says the two bodies are equal in receiving natural motion. This means the receptivity (قابلية) is the same; any difference in motion must come from the power, not from the bodies themselves.
3
Grasp the proportionality of power and effect
Since the difference in substrates is by half (one body is half the other), the powers differ by half, and so do the effects (motions). The effect has no independent variation—it follows the agents.
4
Examine the clarification and objection
The author clarifies that the ratio holds when considering only the bodies themselves. Then he raises an objection: in a medium (like air), the larger body faces more resistance, breaking the ratio. He refutes it by assuming we can adjust the media so resistance is proportional.
This shows the argument assumes idealized conditions, but it is logically valid.
5
Reflect on the significance
This premise is crucial for later arguments about motion and causality. It establishes that power is not a ghostly addition but intimately tied to the physical substrate.
Think about how this premise might be used to argue for something like the existence of an external mover.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the large (regarding the power, i.e., the power divides with the division of the substrate). So the two recipients, meaning the small and large bodies, are equal in receiving natural motion; no difference from their side at all. And the two agents of natural motion, meaning the two powers, differ according to the difference of the substrate. Since the difference of the two substrates is by half, the difference of the two powers is also by half. Thus the difference between their effects is also likewise, since there is no difference in the effect here except by consideration of the difference of the agents. If these two premises are established: the first: He said: (it divides with the division of the substrate because it permeates the whole of it, otherwise the power of a part would be less than that of the whole). Commentary
This passage states and explains the first premise of the philosophers' argument about bodily power. The premise is that the power (al-quwwa) which moves a body naturally divides with the division of its substrate (al-mahall), meaning the power permeates the whole body and is proportional to it. The author first sets up the lemma: the two bodies, small and large, are equal in their receptivity to natural motion; any difference in motion comes only from the moving powers, which differ according to the size of the bodies. Since one body is half the other, the power is also half. The effect (motion) then follows the same ratio. The commentary then explains that this holds when considering only the bodies themselves, ignoring external factors like the medium. It refutes an objection: if motion occurs in a medium, the larger body faces more resistance, but we can assume the media are adjusted so that resistance is proportional, thus preserving the ratio.

Correction: The author argues that we can control for such factors by assuming the media are adjusted so that resistance is proportional, thereby preserving the ratio.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The power of a body in natural motion is proportional to its size, and this proportionality can be maintained even when accounting for medium resistance by assuming suitable adjustments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In which area of your life—your speech, your habits, your relationships—is your faith not actively moving you? The Second Premise: Forced Motion and Resistance
States the second premise about forced motion ratio and its explanation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ratio of forced motion between two bodies of different sizes when moved by the same coercer, and the role of resistance in determining that ratio.

Core Idea
Resistance, not force, determines motion ratio between bodies of different sizes.

This lesson presents the second premise regarding forced motion. The author establishes a key principle: when the same coercive force acts on two bodies of different sizes, their resulting motions are not equal. The ratio depends on the natural resistance of each body. Understanding this premise is essential for later arguments about causality and the nature of physical interactions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents the ratio and then explains the reason.
2
Identify the key components
Identify the agent (coercer), the recipient (bodies), and the resistance. Note the assumed equality in the agent and difference in the recipient.
The author stresses that the difference comes from the recipient, not the agent.
3
Understand the ratio
Understand why the double body moves with half the power of the half. See the commentary explaining that resistance is double, so reception is half.
4
Address the common misunderstanding
Learn what 'half the power of the half' means—it refers to half the power of the double, not half of the smaller body.
This is a common mistake clarified in the commentary.

وقوة الضعف) أي ضعف الجسم (في) قبول التحريك ( القسري ) نصف قوة (النصف) في ذلك القبول، وإنما كانت نسبة القوتين بالنصف (للتساوي) بين الضعف والنصف (في الفاعل فرضاً، بأن نفرض قاسراً واحداً حركهما بقوة واحدة ) والتفاوت في القابل إذ المعاوق ) للحركة القسرية (في الضعف أعني القوة الطبيعية ( العائقة عن قبول الحركة القسرية ( أكثر ) من المعاوق النصف، بحسب زيادة الضعف على النصف، فلا تفاوت حينئذ في الحركة القسرية من جهة الفاعل أصلا بل من جهة القابل في قبوله التفاوت بكثرة المعاوق وقلته، فإذا كانت نسبة المعاوق إلى المعاوق بالضعف كان نسبة القبول إلى القبول بالنصف، فيكون نسبة الأثر إلى الأثر بالنصف أيضاً. قوله : (قوة النصف أي نصف النصف وهو الجسم المفروض ضعفه. قوله : ( بحسب زيادة الضعف إلخ ) بناء على فرض عدم التفاوت في الأمور الخارجة عنهما، وعلى أن ماهية الحركة لا تقتضي قدراً معيناً من الزمان على ما سيجيء في بيان امتناع الخلاء، فلا يرد شبهة أبي البركات هاهنا . قوله : ( كان نسبة القبول إلخ) أي بالنسبة إلى ذات الجسمين؛ لأنا فرضنا التساوي بينهما الأمور الخارجة عنهما . قوله : ( نصف قوة النصف أي نصف الضعف لا نصف الجسم كما يتبادر إلى الوهم .
And the power of the double (i.e., double the body) in receiving forced motion is half the power of the half in that reception. The two powers are in the ratio of half only because of the assumed equality between the double and the half in the agent, by assuming one coercer moving them with one force. The difference is in the recipient, for the resistance to forced motion in the double (i.e., the natural power obstructing the reception of forced motion) is greater than the resistance of the half, according to the increase of the double over the half. So there is no difference in forced motion from the side of the agent at all, but from the side of the recipient in its reception varying by the abundance or scarcity of resistance. Thus if the ratio of resistance to resistance is double, the ratio of reception to reception is half, and so the ratio of effect to effect is also half. He said: (the power of the half means half of the double, i.e., the body assumed to be double its half). He said: (according to the increase of the double etc.) based on assuming no difference in external matters, and on the fact that the nature of motion does not require a specific amount of time, as will come in the explanation of the impossibility of void, so the doubt of Abu al-Barakat does not apply here. He said: (the ratio of reception etc.) meaning with respect to the essence of the two bodies, for we assumed equality between them in external matters. He said: (half the power of the half means half of the double, not half of the body, as might occur to the mind.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage states the second premise concerning forced motion (al-haraka al-qasriyya). The premise is that if one coercer (qasir) applies the same force to two bodies, one double the size of the other, the power required to move the double is half the power required to move the half (because the double has more natural resistance). The ratio of powers is inverse to the ratio of sizes due to resistance. The commentary clarifies that the difference comes from the recipient (al-qabil), not the agent. It also addresses potential objections, such as Abu al-Barakat's doubt about time, and clarifies that 'half the power of the half' means half the power of the double, not half of the smaller body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the coercer's force is divided equally between the two bodies, leading to equal motion.
Correction: The text shows that the ratio depends on the resistance in the recipient, not on the agent's force. The double body has twice the resistance, so its motion is half.

Checkpoint
According to the second premise, if one body is double another and both are moved by the same coercer, what is the ratio of their forced motions?
A) The double moves twice as fast
B) The double moves half as fast
C) They move at the same speed
D) The half moves half as fast

Key Takeaway
The ratio of forced motion between two bodies of different sizes under the same coercer is inversely proportional to their resistances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a trial (a single 'coercer'), do you attribute its effect more to the trial itself or to your inner state? This lesson teaches that the effect depends on the recipient, not the agent. Are you like the double body, with high resistance, or the half body with low resistance? What would it take for you to become more receptive to beneficial hardship?
?
The author dismisses a doubt by clarifying that motion does not require a specific time. What doubts do you hold about God's actions in your life that might be resolved if you understood the true nature of causality? Are you willing to let go of those doubts based on this reasoning?
?
The ratio of forced motion is inversely proportional to resistance. In your spiritual life, your resistance to divine guidance (e.g., arrogance, desires) directly affects how much you benefit from it. How can you reduce your internal resistance today to receive more of God's 'coercion' towards good?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
8 min
The Combined Proof: Infinite Motion is Impossible
Combines both premises to prove motion cannot be infinite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how combining the premises about natural and forced motion leads to the conclusion that bodily power cannot produce infinite motion.

Core Idea
Infinite motion is impossible because it leads to logical contradiction.

In this passage, al-Sanusi brings together the two premises established earlier—the first on natural motion and the second on forced motion—to prove that no bodily power, whether natural or coercive, can generate an infinite series of motions. The argument is a classic demonstration of the impossibility of actual infinity in physical processes, using the relation between powers and motions. Understanding this proof is crucial for the subsequent conclusion that the world must have a beginning in time.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two premises
Before reading, remember that the first premise dealt with natural motion (half power → half motion) and the second with forced motion (coercer can move double).
These premises are essential for the combined proof.
2
Follow the hypothetical assumption
The proof begins by assuming a natural power moves its body infinitely. Then it considers half the body having half the power, starting from the same point.
This sets up the contradiction.
3
Examine the two possibilities for the smaller motion
The motion of the half (in natural) or the double (in forced) is either finite or infinite. The proof shows both lead to impossibility.
The reasoning uses the relation of powers and ratios.
4
Understand the forced motion parallel
The same logic applies to forced motion, using the coercer's ability to move a double body.
This shows the argument is universal for bodily powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا تقرر هاتان المقدمتان الأولى والثانية في الحركة الطبيعية والثانية في الحركة القسرية ( فإذا فرضناهما) أي التحريك الطبيعي والقسري ( من مبدأ واحد ) أي فحينئذ نقول : لا يجوز أن تحرك قوة طبيعية جسمها إلى غير النهاية وإلا فنصف ذلك الجسم له قوة طبيعية هي نصف القوة الطبيعية التي للكل فنفرض أن هاتين القوتين حركتا جسميهما من مبدأ واحد في العدد أو الزمان فلا شك أن حركة النصف نصف حركة الكل؛ لما مر في الأولى . وكذلك نقول : لا يجوز أن تكون قوة جسمانية تحرك جسماً آخر بالقسر إلى غير النهاية، وإلا فلذلك القاسر أن يحرك ضعف ذلك الجسم الآخر، فنفرض أنه حركهما من مبدأ واحد فلا شك أن حركة الضعف نصف حركة النصف لما مر في المقدمة الثانية، فإذا فرضنا ما ذكرنا في الطبيعية والقسرية (فالأقل) وهو حركة النصف في الطبيعية وحركة الضعف في القسرية (إما متناه والأكثر) الذي فرضناه غير متناه ( ضعفه )؛ لما عرفت . ( وضعف المتناهي متناه) بالضرورة، فيكون الأكثر متناهياً، وهو خلاف المفروض، وإما غير متناه، وقد فرضنا مبدأ الأقل والأكثر ( واحداً ، ( فتقع الزيادة عليه ) أي زيادة الأكثر على الأقل ) في الجهة التي هو بها غير متناه، فهو متناه إذ لا بد أن ينقطع في تلك الجهة حتى تتصور الزيادة عليه فيها، ( وأنه ) أي كون الأقل متناهياً في الجهة التي هو فيها غير متناه ( محال ) بالضرورة. قوله : (فحينئذ نقول إلخ) أي حين فرض الحركتين من مبدأ واحد نقول : بالتفصيل في كل واحد منهما هكذا، وخلاصة البرهان في الحركة الطبيعية أنه لو تحرك جسم لقوته الطبيعية حركات غير متناهية وتحرك بعض ذلك الجسم بقوته الطبيعية من مبدأ واحد، فإن كانت حركات البعض غير متناهية وحركات الكل أكثر، وقع التفاوت بين الحركتين في الجانب الغير المتناهي، وإن كانت متناهية يلزم تناهي حركات الكل أيضاً؛ لأن نسبة حركة الكل إلى البعض نسبة قوة الكل إلى قوة البعض، ونسبة القوتين كنسبة الكل إلى البعض، ونسبتهما نسبة المتناهي إلى المتناهي، فيكون نسبة الحركتين نسبة المتناهي إلى المتناهي وقد فرضنا حركة الكل غير متناهية هذا خلف وقس على ذلك برهان القسرية .
If these two premises are established—the first in natural motion and the second in forced motion—then (if we assume them) i.e., the natural and forced motions (from one starting point), i.e., then we say: It is not permissible that a natural power moves its body to infinity; otherwise half of that body has a natural power that is half the natural power of the whole. So we assume that these two powers moved their bodies from one starting point in number or time; no doubt the motion of the half is half the motion of the whole, as passed in the first premise. Similarly, we say: It is not permissible that a bodily power moves another body by force to infinity; otherwise that coercer can move a double of that other body. So we assume that he moved them from one starting point; no doubt the motion of the double is half the motion of the half, as passed in the second premise. So if we assume what we mentioned in natural and forced motion, then the smaller (i.e., the motion of the half in natural, and the motion of the double in forced) is either finite, and the larger which we assumed infinite is its double, as you know. And the double of the finite is necessarily finite, so the larger would be finite, contrary to the assumption; or it is infinite, and we assumed the starting point of the smaller and larger is one, so the increase (i.e., the increase of the larger over the smaller) occurs in the direction in which it is infinite; then it is finite, because it must terminate in that direction for the increase over it to be conceivable. And that (i.e., that the smaller is finite in the direction in which it is infinite) is necessarily impossible. He said: (then we say etc.), i.e., when assuming the two motions from one starting point, we say in detail for each one thus: the summary of the proof in natural motion: if a body moves by its natural power infinite motions, and a part of that body moves by its natural power from one starting point, then if the motions of the part are infinite and the motions of the whole are more, the difference occurs in the infinite side; if they are finite, then the motions of the whole must also be finite, because the ratio of the motion of the whole to the part is as the ratio of the power of the whole to the power of the part, and the ratio of the two powers is as the ratio of the whole to the part, and their ratio is the ratio of finite to finite; thus the ratio of the two motions is the ratio of finite to finite, while we assumed the motion of the whole infinite. This is a contradiction. And analogously for the proof of forced motion.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage presents the combined proof from both premises to show that bodily power cannot produce infinite motion, whether natural or forced. The argument: if a natural power moves its body infinitely, then half the body has half the power. Assuming they start from the same point, the motion of the half is half that of the whole. The whole's motion is assumed infinite, so the half's motion is either finite or infinite. If finite, then the whole's motion (as its double) must also be finite, contradiction. If infinite, then the whole's motion is greater, but since both start from the same point, the increase occurs in the infinite direction, meaning the whole's motion is finite (because to be increased, it must have an end). Both cases lead to impossibility. The same reasoning applies to forced motion using the second premise. The commentary clarifies the logic: the ratio of motions follows the ratio of powers, which are finite, so motion cannot be infinite.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes motion in a vacuum without resistance.
Correction: The commentary addresses that external factors are controlled; the proof relies purely on the relation of powers and motions, not on external conditions.

Checkpoint
In the proof, if the motion of the half is finite, what follows about the motion of the whole?
A) It must also be finite
B) It must be infinite
C) It is half of the half's motion
D) It is indeterminate

Key Takeaway
The combined proof shows that any bodily power, whether natural or forced, cannot produce an infinite series of motions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a rational proof that every created power has a finite limit. Where in your life do you foolishly act as if your own energy, time, or ability will never run out? How does this truth change how you will spend the next 24 hours?
?
If every created force is finite and cannot produce an infinite effect, what does that say about your need for a source of infinite power beyond yourself? In what specific area are you depending on yourself rather than on the Infinite One?
?
The proof shows that infinite motion leads to contradiction. What 'motions' in your life—habits, pursuits, distractions—are you allowing to go on without end, knowing they cannot ultimately satisfy? What one thing will you stop or start before you sleep tonight to realign with reality?
